## Cardiovascular

# Aging-induced Alterations of Contracting Muscle Capillary Hemodynamics

Leonardo F. Ferreira1, Jerermiah B. Williams1, Brad. J. Behnke², Timothy I. Musch, FACSM1, David C. Poole, FACSM1. Kansas State University, Manhattan, KS. 2West Virginia University, Morgantown, WV.

Aging decreases maximal cardiac output and O2 extraction, which contributes to a reduced maximal O2 uptake in old individuals. The decrease in maximal O2 extraction is, in part, a consequence of reduced O2 diffusing capacity (DO2; Fick's Law of Diffusion) which is determined by the structural and functional characteristics of the microcirculation. Structural alterations in the microcirculation cannot explain the reduced DO2 of old individuals. Therefore. understanding of mechanisms underlving the age-induced decrease in DO2 depend on functional assessment of the microcirculation (i.e., capillary hemodynamics) during muscle contractions. PuRposE: To test the hypothesis aging disrupts the capillary hemodynamic response to muscle contractions such that both conductive and diffusive O2 delivery are reduced. METHoDs: We used intravital microscopy to examine the spinotrapezius muscle capillary hemodynamics in 5 Young (Y; 6-8 mo) and 5 old (O; 26-28 mo) F344xBN rats. Twitch muscle contractions (1 Hz, 3-8 V) shown previously to raise VO by 2-4 fold (light/moderate exercise) were elicited for 5 min and microscope images stored on super-VHS tapes (30 frames/s) for offline analysis. We measured red blood cell flux (Frec), velocity (Vrec) and calculated the number of RBCs/capillary length (RBCNo/CAp = Frsc/VRBc) at rest and within 30 s prior to the last muscle contraction. REsuLTS: In young rats muscle contractions increased FrBc (∆ = 29 ± 8 cells/s; P<0.05), VRBc (Δ = 252 ± 68 μm/s; P<0.05) and RBCNo/CAP (Δ = 1.3 ± 0.5 cells/100μm; P<0.05). In marked contrast, capillary hemodynamics of old muscles did not significantly change from rest to contractions [ΔFrBc = -4.4 ± 1.5 cells/s (P=0.99); ΔVRBc = -34 ± 21 μm/s (P=0.95); and ΔRBCno/CAP = -1 ± 0.8 cells/100μm (P=0.90)]. Consequently at the steady state of muscle contractions old muscles displayed lower FrBc (in cells/s, Y: 51 ± 11, O: 32 ± 9; P<0.05) and VRec (in μm/s, Y: 525 ± 94, O: 336 ± 105; P<0.05) during contractions, whereas RBCno/cap (in cells/100μm, Y: 9.7 ± 1, O: 9.5 ± 1 ; P=0.84) was not different compared to young animals. Capillary lineal density was decreased with aging (in caps/mm muscle, Y: 31 ± 3, O: 23 ± 1; P<0.05). Countercurrent flow also tended to be lower in old animals (Y: 21 ± 4%, O: 12 ± 4%; P=0.12). coNcLusIoNs: In old rats there was a lack of increase in O2 delivery and number of RBCs per capillary length during muscle contractions of light-to-moderate intensity. Our data suggest that these are severe microcirculatory derangements in contracting aged muscle that might help explain the reduced perfusive and diffusive (DO2) O2 transport during exercise.

Suppored by AHA (0455582Z), NIH HL-50306 (DCP) and AG-19228 (TIM).

## Cardiovascular

# Effects of Exercise Training on Skeletal Muscle Vascular Geometry in Young and Old Rats

Brad J. Behnke, Michael D. Delp, FACSM. West Virginia University School of Medicine, Morgantown, Wv. Email: bbehnke@hsc.wvu.edu

With aging, there is a reduction in the number of feed arteries perforating the gastrocnemius muscle likely resulting from a reduction in spontaneous physical activity in old rats. We tested the hypothesis that exercise training would ameliorate age-related differences in vascular geometry in skeletal muscle. METHoDs: Young and old male Fischer 344 rats were divided into young sedentary (YS), old sedentary (OS), young exercise-trained (YT) or old exercise-trained (OT) groups, where training consisted of 10- 12 weeks of treadmill exercise. At the end of the training period, all feed arteries perforating the gastrocnemius muscle were isolated, cannulated and maximally dilated for measurement of luminal diameters. REsuLTS: In young rats with exercise training there was a significant increase in the number of feed arteries perforating the gastrocnemius muscle (YS, 8.9±0.1 vs. YT, 10.3±0.2 arteries, p<0.05), whereas exercise training did not affect the number of feed arteries in the aged animals (OS, 7.6±0.2; OT, 7.8±0.2 arteries). However, in old animals there was an increased average luminal cross-sectional area (CSA) in feed arteries with exercise training. DIscussIoN: Contrary to our hypothesis, with exercise training there was no apparent increase in the number of feedarteries perforating the gastrocnemius muscle in old animals. The increased CsA of the feed arteries in old exercise trained animals suggests a structural enlargement of luminal diameters in this group may be a compensatory mechanism to augment blood flow to the muscle. However, the greater number of feed-arteries in young-trained muscle may allow a more precise matching of blood-flow to muscle O2 uptake. Supported by NASA grant NCC2- 1166 and NIH F32 AG25622.

## Cardiovascular

# A Comparison of Ultrasound and Laser Doppler for Measuring Capillary Recruitment Using Models in vitro

Michael G. Clark, Renee M. Ross, John MB Newman, Stephen M. Richards, Stepher Rattigan. University of Tasmania, Hobart, Australia.

## PURPOSE

Contrast enhanced ultrasound (CEU) and laser Doppler flowmetry (LDF) have been used to measure capillary recruitment in vivo in muscle and skin. However, it is imperative that only change in capillary surface area is measured and that measurements are not influenced by change in flow. Accordingly, we compare two methods: CEU and LDF, using artificial capillary tubing systems.

## METHODS

Phospholipid microbubbles, as contrast medium (approx. 2x108/ml, CEU), or red blood cells (3-9% hematocrit, LDF) were used in isotonic saline. Delivery was by syringe pump and CEU measurements were made using a HDI 5o00 with L7-4MHz transducer at a mechanical index of 1.0 that was capable of destroying all bubbles in the ultrasound beam to construct a pulsing interval (time) versus video-intensity curve and calculation of capillary tubing volure (CTV) in the field of interest as well as tubing flow velocity (CTFV). LDF was conducted using a Perimed PeriFlux PF2 operating at 632nm with a Pf 108 probe. For CEU we used microdialysis tubing (280μm) and for LDF, polymer tubes of 250, 100 and 50 um. Measurements were made when tubing diameter was varied with volume delivery rate (VDR) constant. VDR was varied with tubing diameter constant. Tubing capillary surface area was increased by connecting lengths of tubing in zig-zag series, or by flow-sharing with lengths of tubing connected to a manifold (vDR was constant for each, and each pattern was in the field of interest).

## RESULTS

CEU estimates of CTV were not affected by flow rate and increased whether tubing surface area was increased by connecting tubes in series or parallel. CEU measured changes in CTFV followed prediction. The LDF signal decreased as the diameter of the capillary tubes increased at constant VDR. However, the LDF signal increased as the VDR increased and as the surface area was increased by zig-zag lengths of capillary in series, but it decreased overall when surface area was increased with capillary tubes in parallel.

## CONCLUSIONS

These modeling studies indicate that CEU measures changes in capillary recruitment and is not affected by VDR. LDF signal derives mostly from non-vectorial speed and should be used with caution to measure capillary recruitment and may fail to detect capillary recruitment if manifold sharing occurs and mean cell speed falls, CEU can measure increases in capillary surface area that occur in exercise or following insulin action and may provide information as to whether capillary recruitment results from either flow redistribution from short to tortuous capillaries and/or by flow sharing into capillaries of similar properties.

## Cardiovascular

# Exercise Training Improves Peripheral Vascular Function Following Chronic Femoral Artery Occlusion in the Ossabaw Swine Model of Cardiometabolic Risk

Pamela G. Lloyd, Gregory M. Dick, Mouhamad Alloosh, Joseph L. Unthank, Michael Sturek Indiana University, Indianapolis, IN.

PuRposE: Peripheral arterial disease (PAD) is a major cause of morbidity and mortality in the developed world. Diabetes is a strong risk factor for PAD; thus, incidence of PAD can only be expected to rise as the prevalence of diabetes increases. The treatment of choice for PAD is exercise training. Training is well-known to increase collateral-dependent peripheral blood flow in healthy animals, mainly rodents. However, it is unclear whether training induces the same changes in collateral structure and/or function in the setting of the metabolic syndrome. Thus, we investigated peripheral vascular function in Ossabaw swine. Results from this unique large animal model of cardiometabolic risk may be closely relevant to human disease

METHoDS: Swine were divided into 3 groups: sedentary control (C, N=6), sedentary high fat/cholesterol fed (H, N=4), and exercise trained H (HX, N=6). H and HX pigs were fed a high fat/cholesterol diet (46% kcal from fat; 2% cholesterol w:w) for 50 wk. Hydrogenated soybean oil (containing 56% trans fatty acids) served as the fat source. H and HX pigs were obese, hyperlipidemic, hypertensive, and insulin resistant, all characteristics of the metabolic syndrome. HX pias trained on a motorized treadmill 4 d/wk for 7 wk. Pias wore a wireless heart rate monitor for continuous assessment of exercise intensity. After a 10-min warmup, treadmill speed was increased until heart rate reached \~65-75% of max. Pigs then exercised for 30 min, followed by a 5-min cool down. After 4 wk of training, the right femoral artery was occluded under general anesthesia using surgical suture. After recovery, pigs continued training for a further 3 wk. Pigs were then anesthetized for measurement of mean saphenous artery pressure (SAP)

REsuLTS: SAP did not differ between groups in the left (non-occluded) limb. Left SAP fel \~5o% in all aroups in response to acute occlusion of the Ieft femoral artery and did not significantly recover by 10 min post-occlusion. In the right (chronically occluded) limb, SAP was significantly higher in HX than in C pigs (71 ± 5 vs 49 ± 5 mm Hg; p=0.013). Recovery of SAP after 4 wk of femoral occlusion was complete in the HX group (113 ± 14% of pre-occlusion left SAP), and was areater than that in the H group (48 ± 23%; p=0.017 vs HX) or the C group (75 ± 8%, p=NS vs HX). Typical "corkscrew" collaterals were angiographically visible in the right thigh, suggesting that structural remodeling occurred in response to the chronic femoral occlusion.

coNcLusIoNs: Exercise training significantly improves peripheral vascular function in obese Ossabaw swine, a novel and useful large animal model for the study of the effects of exercise on PAD. Support: HL062552, RR013223 (MS)

## Cardiovascular

# The Angiogenic Response to Aerobic Exercise Training is Similar in Aged Compared to Young Men

T P. Gavin, FACSM, R M. Kraus, L M. Westerkamp, J A. Carrithers, C A. Evans, R. S. Ruster, D J. Knapp, J S. McCartney, J P. Garry, R C. Hickner, FACSM. East Carolina University, Greenville, NC. Email: gavint@ecu.edu

Advanced age is associated with an attenuated skeletal muscle angiogenic response to limb ischemia. We have previously reported that the vascular endothelial growth factor (VEGF) mRNA response to acute aerobic exercise is attenuated in aged men and women. VEGF is necessary for exercise-induced skeletal muscle angiogenesis. purposE: To determine if the skeletal muscle anqiogenic response to aerobic exercise traininq is attenuated in aged versus young men. METHoDs: Six young (25.3 ± 1.4 yrs) and 8 aged (65.1 ± 2.3 yrs) men completed 8 weeks of cycle ergometer exercise training (60 min/day, 4 days/week at 65% of VO2max). Vastus lateralis muscle biopsies were obtained prior to and after completion of the training program. Standard morphological techniques were used to analyze capillarization. REsuLTS: VOmax was lower in aged men regardless of training status and was increased by exercise training similarly between young and aged. Capillary contacts (CC) surrounding Type I and IIa fibers were increased similarly in young and aged by exercise training. CC surrounding type IIa and type IIb fibers were lower in aged regardless of training status, coNcLusIoNs: These results suggest that the skeletal muscle angiogenic response to exercise training is preserved with advanced age, which is in contrast to the attenuated response to limb ischemia. It is likely that the regulation of skeletal muscle angiogenesis is different between physiologically and pathologically inducec stimulation.

Supported by: NIA (AG-21891 to TP Gavin), AHA Mid-Atlantic (0465415U to TP Gavin), and NIH (AG-19209 to RC Hickner)

## Cardiovascular

# Resting Microvascular Exchange is Increased After 7-days of Exercise Training in Young and Older, Men and Women

Raymond M. Kraus, John A. Carrithers, Timothy P. Gavin, FACSM, Rebecca S. Ruster, Deborah ]. Knapp, Christopher A. Evans, Joseph P. Garry, Robert C. Hickner, FACSM. East Carolina University, Greenville, NC.

Aging is associated with a reduction in resting limb blood flow as well as resting skeletal muscle microvascular exchange. However, whether exercise training can improve skeletal muscle microvascular exchange in young and older individuals has yet to be explored PuRposE: To determine if 7-d of moderate intensity cycle ergometry exercise training can enhance resting skeletal muscle microvascular exchange in young and older participants METHoDs: Before and after 7-d of cycle ergometry exercise training (65% VOmax; \~1- h/d) microdialvsis probes were utilized to determine resting microvascular exchange in the vastus lateralis of sedentary, young (men; 25.5 ± 4.3y, n = 8: women; 23.8 ± 3.5y, n = 6, mean ± se) and older (men; 64.3 ± 2.3y, n = 8: women; 66.0 ± 7.6y, n = 6) individuals. The microdialysis ethanol technique employed yields an ethanol outflow/inflow (o/i) ratio which is inversely related to microvascular exchange within skeletal muscle. REsuLTs: When analyzing the ethanol o/i ratios a main effect for exercise training (p = 0.045) was observed, indicating that a significant decrease in o/i ratio (increase in microvascular exchange) had resulted from 7-d of exercise training. An age by gender interaction (p = 0.018) was detected: upon close inspection of the data it is apparent that, independent of exercise training, the young women have a lower o/i ratio (higher resting microvascular exchange) than older women. There was no such difference in o/i ratio between young men and older men. No significance for the training state by age interaction (p = 0.13) was found. coNcLUsIoNs: These data demonstrate that 7-d of moderate intensity cycle ergometry exercise training does increase resting microvascular exchange in young and older, men and women. Addition of participants to increase statistical power may yield a significant training state by age interaction, in that there was little effect of training on microvascular exchange in the older compared to the younger participants. Supported by NIH AG-19209 (RC Hickner) and NIA AG-21891 (TP Gavin).

## Cardiovascular

# Effect of Brief Exercise on Circulating Stem Cell Mobilization Factors in Children

Frank Zaldivar, Jr., Shlomit Aizik-Radom, Lori Wilson, Dan M. Cooper. University of California, Irvine, Irvine, CA. Email: fpzaldiv@uci.edu

PURPosE: We recently presented preliminary data demonstrating that brief, moderate exercise (designed to mimic naturally occurring physical activity in children) increased circulating stem cells (CD34+) in early- and late pubertal (EP, LP) boys. We hypothesized that exercise would also increase circulating mediators that mobilize CD34+, namely, Fms like tyrosine kinase-3 (FLT-3), interleukin 3 (IL-3), Stromal cell-Derived Factor-α 1(SDFa-1), Granulocyte Colony Stimulating Factor (G-CSF), and Granulocyte Macrophage Colony Stimulating Factor (GM-CSF)

METHODS: Eight EP (10.2 ± 0.41 y.0.) and 8 LP (16.7 ± 0.74 y.0.) boys performed 20 min of moderate (\~ 70% peak) cycle ergometer exercise. Blood was drawn before and immediately following exercise. Cells were stained for CD34+ using ISHAGE definition (i.e., cells gated on CD45 + dim /low side scatter CD34† positive cells). Samples were acquired using a FACS Calibur flow cytometer (Becton Dickinson, San Jose, CA). FLT-3, IL-3, SDFa 1, G-CSF, and GM-CSF were measured in plasma usinq commercially available ELISA kits. REsuLTS: CD34+ cells increased from 112 ± 21 cells/μl to 182 ± 30 at end-exercise in EP (p<0.001), and from 63 ± 8 to 153 ± 21 in LP (p<0.001). EP baseline values of CD34+, FLT-3 (97.80 ± 5.01 pg/ml), and G-CSF (26.16 ± 4.09 pg/ml) were significantly greater than LP baseline values of CD34+, FLT-3 (72.72 ± 5.66), and G-CSF (13.68 ± 1.25). Exercise led to significant increases in FLT-3 in EP (+12.30 ± 1.63) and LP (+19.11 ± 1.88) and the LP changes were significantly larger. Exercise led to significant increases in G-CSF in EP (+3.33 ± 0.59) and LP (+4.36 ± 0.91), with no significant difference betweer the groups.

coNcLusIoNs: Brief moderate exercise in children not only increases circulating stem cells, but also raises FLT-3 and G-CSF. Moreover, both at baseline and in the response to exercise, pubertal status influences the levels of these requlatory mediators. Whether or not the greater responses to exercise in I P indicate an increased ability to ultimately adapt to physical activity, or, alternatively, parallels the generally larger metabolic response to heavy exercise (e.g., we found higher lactate levels in LP compared with EP) is not known.

# MRI-Measured Blood Oxygen Level-Dependent (BOLD) Transients in Skeletal Muscle After Brief Contractions in Healthy Elderly Subjects

Theodore F. Towse, Jill M. Slade, Ronald A. Meyer. Michigan State University, East Lansing, MI. Email: towsethe@msu.edu

INTRoDuCTION: A maqnetic resonance imaqing (MRI) technique sensitive to changes in the blood oxygen level. blood volume and microvascular density has recently been used to study the functional status of the heart, kidney and skeletal muscle. In a large crosssectional study age was a strong predictor of the peak changes in the BoLD transients in individuals matched for physical activity and BM] (1). PURPOSE: To determine the magnitude of MRI measured BOLD transients in healthy, elderly subjects

SUBJECTS: 11 healthy (3 female) elderly (76 yr., Ht 171cm, Wt 77 kg) subjects. METHoDS: MRI images were acquired with a clinical extremity coil on a 1.5 T GE Horizor system. T1-weighted (3-Plane localizer) and T2-weighted (axial fast-spin-echo) images were acguired to locate the largest cross-sectional area of the anterior tibialis muscle. One shot gradient-recalled echo-planer images were acquired from a single axial slice transecting the largest cross-sectional area of the tibialis anterior muscle. Images were acauired continuously for 4 min, durina which time subjects performed a single, 1-s duration maximal isometric ankle dorsiflexion every 30 s (7 contractions). The time-course of signal intensity (SI) changes in the anterior compartment from manually demarked regions-of-interest, drawn with care taken to exclude any vessels. The peak change in SI above baseline was calculated for the post-contractile transients as described previously (2).

RÉsULTS: Typical MR measured postcontractile response for an elderly subject as shown in the figure. The mean response for the elderly subjects was 0.5 %. This is compared to a mean response of 1.5 % for young, healthy sedentary subjects in a previous study (2).

CONCLUSION: MR measured postcontractile transients are nearly absent in healthy, elderly subjects. As this technique is sensitive to changes in blood volume and

## Cardiovascular

# Impaired Arteriolar Blood Flow with Aging in Skeletal Muscle of Male C57BI/6 Mice

Dwayne N. Jackson1, Steven S. Segal, FACsM². 1University of Western Ontario, London ON. Canada, 2University of Missouri - Columbia, Columbia, MO Email: dwayne.jackson@neuropeptide.com

Skeletal muscle blood flow and exercise capacity are diminished with aging but little is known of the underlving changes in microvascular hemodynamics, In second-order arterioles (2A) of the qluteus maximus muscle of old (> 20 month) male C57Bl/6 mice (OM), we recently reported (FASEB J. 20: A271, 2006) that the "Rapid Onset of Vasodilation" in response to a single tetanic contraction (100 Hz, 100-1000 ms) was blunted by nearly 50% relative to that in young (3 month) males (YM). However, 2A dilations in response to 30 seconds of rhythmic contractions at 2, 4, and 8 Hz were not different between age-groups, nor were resting (\~18 μm) or maximum (\~30 μm; 10 μM sodium nitroprusside, SNP) 2A luminal diameters. In the present study, we hypothesized that blood flow in 2A would be impaired in OM vs. YM. The gluteus maximus muscle of pentobarbital-anesthetized C57Bl/6 mice (n=6 per age-group) was prepared for intravital microscopy to measure 2A red blood cell velocity and lumen diameter in order to calculate blood flow at rest and in response to single tetanic and rhythmic contractions (as above). In OM vs. YM, respectively, heart rates (353 ± 22 vs. 378 ± 15 beats/min) and carotid arterial blood pressures (76 ± 3 vs. 76 ± 1 mmHg) were similar. However, respective 2A blood flows at rest (0.6 ± 0.1 vs. 1.6 ± 0.2 nl/s) and during maximum dilation with SNP (2.0 ± 0.8 vs. 5.4 ± 0.8 nl/s) were attenuated by more than 60% (P<0.05) with aging. Blood flow in 2A increased with the duration of tetanic contraction in YM (by 0.4 ± 0.2 to 2.1 ± 0.5 nl/s) and these responses were blunted by more than 70% in OM (0.1 ± 0.1 to 0.4 ± 0.1 nl/s; P<0.05). For rhythmic contractions, 2A blood flow increased with stimulation frequency for both age-groups but peak responses in YM (2.3 ± 0.6 to 3.5 ± 0.8 nl/s) were 2- to 3-fold greater (P<0.05) than in OM (0.7 ± 0.2 to 1.5 ± 0.3 nl/s) despite similar 2A dilations. We conclude that aging blunts arteriolar blood flow in mouse skeletal muscle at rest and during responses to muscle contractions. We hypothesize that this effect occurs through an increase in the resistance of vessel segments upstream from 2A branches. (Acknowledgements: Supported by Nationa! Institutes of Health grants AG19347 & HL56786 to SSS and by a postdoctoral fellowship to DN] from the Heart and Stroke Foundation of Canada. These experiments were performed at The John B. Pierce Laboratory, Yale University School of Medicine, New Haven, CT)

## Cardiovascular

# Effects of Training on Postprandial Flow Mediated Dilation

Jennifer L. Olive, Kevin D. Ballard, James Miller, James Robinson. University of Louisville, Louisville, KY. Email: jloliv05@gwise.louisville.edu

The purpose of this study was to determine if reqular endurance training (3-6 years) has a protective effect on vascular endothelial function following the consumption of a high-fat meal. Healthy, young males (21 ± 2 years) were classified as trained (n = 10, 75 ± 5 ml/kg/min) or untrained (n = 10, 47 ± 7 ml/kg/min) as determined by oxygen consumption. Body composition was measured via bioelectrical impedance analysis to determine fat-free mass and fat mass. Flow mediated dilation (FMD) of the brachial artery following reactive hyperemia was determined before, 2-, and 4-hours after the consumption of high-fat meal using Doppler ultrasound. Blood was drawn to determine fasting and postprandial blood lipid levels. The high fat meal was a typical breakfast meal from a fast food restaurant and was composed of 1050 calories, 66 g fat, 21.5 g saturated fat, and 330 mg cholesterol. FMD prior to the high fat meal (approx. 9%) was not significantly different between groups (p = 0.398). FMD was significantly reduced at 2- (35% and 36% for trained and untrained, respectively; p < 0.001) and 4-hours (21% and 36% for trained and untrained, respectively; p < 0.001) postprandial compared to baseline. The FMD response was not significantly different between groups after the meal at either time point (p = 0,119). Serum TG increased at 2- (45% and 79% for trained and untrained, respectively; p < 0.001) and 4-hours (55% and 92% for trained and untrained, respectively; p < 0.001) from baseline in both groups. LDL-C was reduced at 4-hours (6% p = 0.05) in trained subjects, and 2- and 4-hours (19% and 11%, respectively; p < 0.01) in untrained subjects from baseline. HDL-C was reduced at 2-hours (1.6% and 4.9% in trained and untrained, respectively; p = 0.024) in both groups but returned back to baseline levels by 4-hours. No significant differences were found between groups on any lipid marker at any time point. Trained individuals exhibited smaller changes in blood lipids than the untrained individuals; however, these were not significant. FMD was not sianificantly correlated to the blood lipid levels at baseline, 2-, or 4-hours, In conclusion. regular endurance training does not prevent transient reductions in vascular function for up to 4-hours after the ingestion of a single high-fat meal

11

## Cardiovascular

# Mechanical Influences on Skeletal Muscle Vascular Tone in Humans: Insights into Contraction-Induced Rapid Vasodilation

Brett S. Kirby. Rick F. Carlson, Rachel M. Markwald. Frank A. Dinenno, Colorado State University, Fort Collins, CO. Email: kirbs@cahs.colostate.edu

Recent evidence indicates that mechanical deformation of rat skeletal muscle feed arteries elicits a rapid vasodilation that peaks in \~4-5 seconds, strikingly similar to the vasodilation observed in response to a sinale muscle contraction in vivo. Further, this vasodilator response appears to be sensitive to repeated vessel deformation rather than sustained deformation. PuRPosE: We tested the hypothesis that mechanical deformation via acute increases in extravascular pressure elicits rapid vasodilation in humans, that the time-topeak dilation is similar to that observed in response to muscle contractions, and that the vasodilatory response is more sensitive to repeated than sustained deformation in vivo METHoDs: We measured forearm blood flow (Doppler ultrasound) and calculated forearm vascular conductance (FvC) in response to whole forearm compression in 5 young healthy adults. Forearm compressions were performed using a rapid cuff inflation unit and a custom-designed blood pressure cuff with the arm above heart level at 100 and 200 mmHa in the following manner: Condition 1: a single 2 second elevation in cuff pressure; Condition 2: a single 6 second elevation in cuff pressure; Condition 3: five repeated 2 second elevations in cuff pressure separated by 1 second of deflation. Isometric handgrip exercise was performed in a similar fashion at 30% maximum voluntary contraction to match the peak vasodilator response observed in Condition 1 of forearm compressions REsuLTS: In Condition 1, brief forearm compression of 100 and 200 mmHg evoked a significant and graded vasodilation (peak ΔFVC = \~130% and \~200%, respectively). In contrast to our hypothesis. sustained forearm compressions (Condition 2) evoked the greatest vasodilation (ΔFVC = \~190% and \~240% to 100 and 200 mmHg), whereas repeated forearm compressions (Condition 3) only resulted in \~115% and \~110% increase in FVC. For isometric muscle contractions, the peak increase in FVC was \~225% for Condition 1, \~290% for Condition 2. and \~410% for Condition 3. Time-to-peak vasodilation for all forearm compression trials occurred within 2 cardiac cycles following cuff deflation, whereas that observed following muscle contractions was at \~4-5 cardiac cycles. coNcLusIoNs: Mechanical deformation via acute increases in extravascular pressure elicits rapid vasodilation in the human forearm, but this does not appear more sensitive to repeated than sustained vascular deformation. Importantly, given that the peak vasodilator response after forearm compression occurs within 2 cardiac cycles, mechanical influences cannot fully explain the peak vasodilation observed in response to brief muscle contractions.

12

## Cardiovascular

# Aging Decreases NOs Regulatory Mechanisms in Skeletal Muscle Resistance Arteries

Amanda J. LeBlanc1, Brad J. Behnke1, Guoyao Wu², Judy M. Muller-Delp², Michael D. Delp1. West Virginia University, Morgantown, WV. 2Texas A&M University, College Station, TX.

Previous data demonstrate that endothelium-dependent vasodilation decreases with age in soleus muscle resistance arteries despite an increase in eNos protein expression. Decreased levels of the cofactor tetrahydrobiopterin (BH4) or limited intracellular L-arginine availability, possibly via increased arginase activity, could lead to reduced bioavailability of NO in skeletal muscle resistance arteries. Therefore, the purpose of this study was to determine whether NO bioavailability is lower in soleus muscle resistance arteries as a result of age-related alterations in the NOS signaling pathway. Arginase activity, L-arginine and BH4 levels, and intraluminal NO concentration were evaluated in isolated soleus muscle resistance arteries from young (4 mo) and old (24 mo) male Fisher-344 rats. The effect of arginase inhibition on vasodilation was determined by evaluating flow-induced vasodilation in soleus muscle resistance arteries in the presence and absence of N?-hydroxy-nor-Larginine (NOHA). The old age-associated decrement in flow-induced vasodilation was not abolished with arginase inhibition, and L-arginine concentrations did not differ between soleus muscle resistance arteries from young and old rats, indicating substrate availability is not limited with aging. In contrast, BH4 levels were \~ 50% lower in soleus muscle resistance arteries from old rats (0.23 ± 0.02 vs 0.09 ± 0.01 pmol/mg tissue; young vs old). Correspondingly, basal, ACh- and bradykinin-induced NO production were lower in arteries from old rats. These results indicate that although eNOS protein is increased with age in skeletal muscle resistance arteries, No levels are lower and this is associated with age-related reductions in BH4 bioavailability. In addition, contrary to that found in large conduit arteries, arginase inhibition did not alter vasodilation in resistance arteries from old rats, likely due to the different physicochemical environment of resistance vessels versus conduit arteries.

13

## Cardiovascular

# Effect of Limb Immobilization on Venous Properties in Healthy Subjects

Nicolas Tordi. EA 3920 Physiopathologie cardio-vasculaire et prevention, Besancon, France Email: nicolas.tordi@univ-fcomte.fr

From an experimental perspective, various situations have commonly been used to assess the effects of deconditioning on peripheral vasculature. Lower limb suspension (LLS) provides an interestina model to induce muscle inactivity and underlving mechanisms contributing to vascular adaptation . PuRposE: However, the effect of inactivity through LLS on venous hemodynamic characteristics has not been extensively studied.

METHoDS: Healthy subjects, (6 males 2 females age: 21.6 ± 1 [mean±SE]) were tested before and after 2 weeks of unilateral LLS. Venous characteristics of the right and left calf were assessed using venous occlusion plethysmography. Venous occlusions of 20, 40, 60, and 80 mmHg, separated by 1-min breaks, were sustained for 2, 3, 4, and 5 min respectively. To assess venous adaptations, venous compliance between the two medium occlusion pressures and half empty time (Tiv2) after occlusion at 40 mmHg, were calculated for each leg from the pressure/volume curves.

REsuLTS: The pressure/yolume curve of the immobilized leg was reduced, with a concomitant reduction in calf volume (2380.5 ± 147.5 vs. 2219 ± 150.8 cm² ; - 7%) and a significant decrease in venous compliance (0.062 ± 0.004 vs. 0.042 ± 0.003 ml.dl 1.mmHg"1) whereas T1/2 was unaltered. None of these adaptations were observed in the non-immobilized leg

coNcLusIoN: These findings suggest that filling and emptying parameters, which characterize venous herodynamic properties, are unequally affected by muscle inactivity. A functional link between adaptations in venous dimension and muscle activity anc consequently blood flow may be suggested although the mechanism and their mutual dependency in humans is still a largely unexplored area.

. Sugawara J, Hayashi K, Kaneko F, Yamada H, Kizuka T, and Tanaka H. Reductions ir basal limb blood flow and lumen diameter after short-term leg casting. Med Sci Sports Exerc, 2004; 36: 1689-94.

2. Bleeker MW, De Groot PC, Poelkens F, Rongen GA, Smits P, and Hopman MT. Vascular adaptation to 4 wk of deconditioning by unilateral lower limb suspension. Am J Physiol Heart Circ Physiol. 2005; 288: H1747-55. Epub 2004 Dec 2

## Cardiovascular

# The Effect of Contraction Frequency on the Central and Peripheral Blood Flow / VO2 Relationship

Barbara J. Lutjemeier², Craig A. Harms, FACSM², Dana K. Townsend², Leonardo F. Ferreira', Allison 1. Harper², Thomas J. Barstow, FACsM1. Kansas State University, Manhattan, KS. 2University of Toledo, Toledo, OH. Email: bjl1341@ksu.edu

The blood flow / vO, relationship has been shown to be linear and robust at both the central (i.e., cardiac output / pulmonary VO,) and peripheral (leq blood flow / leg VO2) levels. However, an increase in contraction frequency has been reported to either decrease, have no effect, or increase the blood flow response during exercise, potentially altering this relationship. PURPOSE: To determine if the steady state coupling between muscle blood flow and metabolic rate (centrally and peripherally) would be altered by varying contraction frequency (pedal rate) during upright cycle exercise. METHODS: Six healthy subjects performed discontinuous ramp protocols (5 min. stages) on a cycle ergometer at either 60 or 100 rpm with 90 minutes of rest between protocols. Pulmonary gas exchange was measured breath-by-breath. Cardiac output, measured as pulmonary blood flow (COp), was determined by the single breath acetylene absorption technique. Leg blood flow (LBF) was measured by the constant-infusion thermodilution technique and leg VO2 was calculated as the product of leg blood flow and arteriovenous O2 content difference across the leg. RESULTS: Central findings: both CO, and VO2 increased linearly with power output. Results from paired t-test showed no significant difference (p=0.39) between the slopes of COp vs. VO2 at 60rpm (4.95 ± 0.91) compared to 100rpm (5.23 ± 0.37). Peripheral findings: both LBF and leg VO2 increased linearly with power output. There was no significant difference (p=0.37) between the slopes of LBF vs. leg VO at 60rpm (5.79 ± 0.40) compared to 100rpm (5.50 ± 0.39). CONCLUSIONS: These results indicate that the central and peripheral blood flow / VO relationship is robust and remains tightly coupled regardless of changes in contraction freguency. Further, muscle blood flow does not become limited at higher contraction frequencies for this mode of exercise. Supported by AHA Grant-in-Aid 0151183Z.

# Effects of Age and Exercise Training on Nitric Oxide Bioavailability in Cerebral Resistance Arteries

Rafael A. Reyes², Kathryn E. Nichol², Scott A. Spier², Amanda LeBlanc³, Judy Muller-Delp3 1University of the Andes/West Virginia University, Mérida-Venezuela/Morgantown, WV Texas A&M University, College Station, TX. 3West Virginia Univ., Morgantown, WV.

Previous work has shown that endothelium-dependent dilation is maintained in cerebra resistance arteries with age: however. the mechanisms that regulate nitric oxide bioavailability may be altered by age and exercise training. PuRPosE: The purpose of this study was to determine the effects of age and exercise training on mechanisms that regulate endothelial nitric oxide availability in cerebral resistance arteries. METHoDs: Young (4 mo) and old (24 mo) male Fischer-344 rats underwent exercise training or remained sedentary for 10 weeks. Vasodilation to acetylcholine (ACh) was determined ir posterior communicating arteries (PCoA) under control conditions and during treatment with 1-(2-trifluoromethylphenyl) imidazole (TRIM) and aminoquanidine (AG) to block neuronal nitric oxide synthase (nNos) and inducible nitric oxide synthase (iNos), respectively. Responsiveness to ACh was also evaluated in the presence and absence of Tiron, a cell perrmeable form of superoxide dismutase. REsuLTs: In PCoA from both young and old rats, non-specific blockade of NOS with NG-nitro-L-arginine methyl ester (L-NAME) eliminated dilation to ACh (p=0.0001). Simultaneous treatment with TRIM and AG eliminated ACh-induced dilation in PCoA from both sedentary and exercise trained old rats; however, significant dilation remained in the presence of TRIM and AG in young sedentary and exercise trained rats (p=0.003 and p=0.0001; respectively). Tiron enhancec vasodilation to ACh in PCoA from both sedentary and exercise trained old rats (p=0.008 and p= 0.oo3; respectively), but not in PCoA from young rats, regardless of training status. coNcLusIoNs: These results, along with similar measurements of basal and AChstimulated NO production in PCoA from young and old rats, suggest the bioavailability of NO in PCoA decreases with age possibly due to an increase in superoxide. Furthermore, these data indicate that exercise training upregulates eNOS activity in PCoA of young but not old rats. In PCoA of old rats, the ability of exercise training to increase eNOS activity may be limited by the presence of greater oxidant stress. Supported by NIH R01HL077224 and AHA GIA0255956Y

# Sex Differences in Splanchnic Hemodynamics during 70 Head-Up Tilt

Sara S. Jarvis, John P. Florian, Michael J. Curren, James A. Pawelczyk, FACSM Pennsylvania State University, University Park, PA

PuRposE: To determine the effect of the somatostatin analog octreotide acetate, a selective splanchnic vasoconstrictor, on tilt table tolerance and splanchnic vascular conductance in women (W) and men (M). METHoDS: Heart rate (HR), blood pressure (MAP), cardiac index (CI; cardiac output/surface area), and splanchnic vascular conductance (SpVC; splanchnic blood flow/MAP) were measured during: baseline (B), post infusion (PI) of placebo (Pla) or octreotide (Oct, 100-125 μg i.v.), and 70° head-up til (HUT) in 14 W (23±2 yrs; mean±SE) and 16 M (23±1 yrs). Kaplan-Meier survival analysis and Mood's median test were used to assess differences in tilt time between the sexes and treatments. Hemodynamic variables were assessed by ANOvA (sex x stage x treatment). REsuLTS: Survival to 45 min during 70° HUT and median time to presyncope were not statistically different between the sexes with Pla (15.7 vs. 21.8 min, p=0.09, W and M, respectively) or Oct (37.0 vs. 45.0 min, p=NS). Oct increased time to presyncope in both sexes (p<0.05); 0% of W completed 45 min of HUT with Pla and 50% with Oct, while 19% of M completed 45 min of HUT with Pla and 63% with Oct. HR was not different between sexes or treatments (B: 65±2 vs. 60±2 bpm; HUT: 96±3 vs. 94±3 bpm). MAP was lower in W at B (77±1 vs. 86±2 mmHg, p<0.05) and HUT (72±2 vs. 83±2 mmHg, p<0.05) with Pla; this difference did not exist with the administration of Oct. SpVC was lower (p<0.05) in W compared to M during rest but not HUT in both trials, Oct elicited splanchnic vasoconstriction during HUT as SpVC was reduced (p<0.05) in both sexes (Pla: 13.1±1.2 vs. 15.6±1.3 ml/min/mmHg, Oct: 10.4±0.6 vs. 12.3±0.9 ml/min/mmHg). W demonstrated a higher CI than M during HUT (Pla: 2.67±0.22 vs. 2.28±0.13 L/min/m², Oct: 2.91±0.21 vs. 2.42±0.15 L/min/m²; p<0.05), and no significant change in CI between B and HUT with the administration of Oct (p= 0.06). Higher CI with a concomitant decrease in SpVC during the Oct trial suggests that in W MAP was maintained during HUT by mobilizing blood away from the splanchnic region, augmenting cardiac preload. coNcLusioNs: W exhibit lower MAP during HUT and tend to have lower tilt-table tolerance. Administration of octreotide acetate improves tilt table tolerance by constrictina the splanchnic reaion (decreasing SpVC), leading to increases in CI and subsequently, maintenance of MAP. Supported by NASA NNJ04HF45G and NIH MO1RR10732

# Determinants of Percentage of Predicted Exercise Capacity in Premenopausal Women: A MONET Study

Joseph Abdulnour1, Bernard Pinet1, Pierre Boulay², Eric Doucet², Martin Brochu³, Rémi Rabasa-Lhoret, Denis Prud'homme². University of Ottawa, Ottawa, ON, Canada. 2Montfort Hospital, Ottawa, ON, Canada. "Université de Sherbrooke, Sherbrooke, PQ, Canada 4Université de Montréal, Montréal, PQ, Canada

PURPOSE: Many studies have shown that cardiovascular disease is the first cause of mortality in women. Exercise capacity has been demonstrated to be an independent predictor of the risk of death and cardiac events among asymptomatic women. It has been reported that women with an exercise capacity of less than 85% of the age-predicted value are at a higher risk (hazard ratio of 2.44) of death from cardiac causes than those with at least 85% of the age-predicted value (Gulati et al. 1). The objective of this study was to investigate the relationships between body composition, metabolic indices and the percentage of predicted exercise capacity for a given age, in asymptomatic premenopausal women. METHODS: A total of 97 premenopausal women (age: 49.9±2.0 yrs; % fat: 31.1±6.5 % mean±sd) underwent a stress-test to measure maximal exercise capacity (VO2 peak) (VO2: 33.9±5.7 mlO2•kg"1•min"1). A nomogram established by Gulati et al. (1) was used to determine the % of the predicted exercise capacity achieved (%PEC) (103.1±14.2 %) according to their age and the measured VO peak. Body composition was measured by DEXA and abdominal fat by axial computed tomography. Other measurements included blood pressure, fasting blood samples for plasma lipids, insulin and glucose levels. RESULTS: Body mass index (r=-0.32, P<0.01), waist circumference (r=- 0.24, P<0.05), %fat (r=-0.58, P<0.01), fat-free mass (r=0.37, P<0.01), fat mass (r=- 0.47, P<0.01), triglycerides (r=-0.29, P<0.01), total cholesterol/HDL-C ratio (r=-0.30, P<0.01), insulin (r=-0.29, P<0.01), and glucose (r=-0.21, P<0.05) were all significantly related to %PEC. Abdominal subcutaneous, visceral or total areas of adipose tissue accumulation and blood pressure were not correlated with %PFC. A stepwise multiple regression analysis showed that % fat (β=-0.59, P<0.001) and plasma triglycerides (β=- 0.32, P<0.001) were significantly related to the inter-individual variation observed in %PEC (R²=0.44, p<0.001). CONCLUSION: In conclusion, our results showed that % fat and plasma concentration of fasting triglyceride account for 44% of the inter-individua variation observed in the %PEC in our sample of asymptomatic premenopausal women. (1) Gulati, M., Black, H. R., Shaw, L. J., Arnsdorf, M. F., Merz, C. N. B., & Lauer, M. S. et al. (2o05). The prognostic value of a nomogram for exercise capacity in women. New England Journal of Medicine, 353(5), 468-475.

# Comparison of ECG Signal Quality between a Novel Dry Electrode and a Standard Gel Electrode

Aaron Rood², K Sparks². 1Orbital Research INC, Cleveland, OH. 2Cleveland State University Cleveland, OH. Email: rood@orbitalresearch.com

BACKGROuND: Electrocardiography has an important role in diagnosis and monitoring of patients. Surface electrodes relay signals from the patient to the electrocardiograph device Traditionally gel based electrodes are used to transmit the electrical signal. However, gel electrodes are inconvenient because they require aggressive skin preparation and use an electrolytic solution to ensure electrocardiograph (ECG) quality. The purpose of this study was to compare the reliability and quality of signal of the traditional gel electrode with a novel dry electrode that requires no abrasive skin preparation or electrolytic solution. The dry electrode is designed with micro-anchors that penetrate the SC layer of skin. The micro-anchors are intended to reduce motion artifact, increase patient comfort and maintain signal quality relative to traditional electrodes. The sensing element, housing and snap are monothilic polymer plated with Ag/AgCl- for conductivity. A novel adhesive scheme is used to maintain the dry electrode skin interface. The tape is clear, flexible and breathable. SUBJECTS: Eleven apparently healthy subjects consisting of 6 males and 5 females volunteered as subject (30.25 +/- 7.8 years). All subjects signed a subject consent form approved by the Cleveland State University Institutional Review Board METHODS: Resting and exercise ECGs were obtained using standard ECG telemetry equipment (ScottCare TeleAdvantage System-Cleveland, Ohio). Subjects were tested on two consecutive days, wearing electrode either the novel dry electrode or the standard 3M Red dot electrode a lead II configuration. The electrode testing sequence was done in randomized sequence. Data was collected in the supine, standing and during a submaximal Bruce protocol (GXT) ECG signals for the entire data set were collected at 120 Hz and were analyzed using the Matlab a 6th order Debauchy wavelet technique to de-noise the traces and establish a signal to noise ratio. RESULTS: A two tailed paired t-test was performed on the signal to noise ratios to compare the signal quality of the two types of electrodes. The mean signal to noise ratio was 16.33 ±2.68 for the standard gel electrode and 17.29 ±2.06 for the dry electrode with a nonsignificant difference (P value of 0.2573). CoNCLUSIONS: It was concluded that the novel dry electrode provided signal quality that was comparable to the standard gel electrode without the need of skin preparation or electrolytic solution. The novel dry electrode may be a suitable alternate to gel based electrodes for the ECG studies. Additional studies are pending to exploit the fidelity of the novel dry electrodes in more chronic applications.

# Relation of Heart Rate Recovery After Maximal Arm Exercise Testing to Resting Heart Rate Variability in Persons with Spinal Cord Injury

Sae Young Jae, Kevin S. Heffernan, Miyoung Lee, Weimo Zhu, FACSM, Bo Fernhall, FACSM University of Illinois at Urbana & Champaign, Champaign, IL. Email: jae@ujuc.edu

PURPosE: The relationship between heart rate recovery (HRR) after treadmill exercise testing and resting cardiac autonomic activity is well known in persons without disabilities Because of differences in autonomic modulation between arm and leg exercise, we investigated the relationship between HRR after maximal arm exercise testing and resting autonomic activity in persons with spinal cord injury (SCI)

METHoDs: Frequency domain analyses of heart rate variability were obtained for a 3-min resting period in 17 subjects with SCI (23.4±3.3 yrs). Absolute values attained were normalized for changes in total power. Normalized high frequency (HF, 0.15-0.40 Hz) power was used an index of parasympathetic activity while the LF/HF ratio was taken as an indicator of sympathovagal balance. Subjects then completed a maximal arm cycle ergometry test with gas exchange measurements, HRR was calculated as the difference between maximum heart rate during the test and heart rate 1 minute (HRR1) and 2 (HRR2) minutes after cessation of exercise.

RESULTS: HRR 1 and HRR 2 was significantly correlated with HF (r=0.46, p<0.05 and r=0.47, p<0.05), LF (r=-0.43, p<0.05 and r=-0.44, p<0.05) and the LF/HF ratio (r=-0.49, p<0.05 and r=-0.50, p<0.05). After adjusting for age, maximal heart rate and vO2max. HRR2 was still significantly associated with HF (r=0.50, p<0.05), LF (r=-0.46, p<0.05) and the LF / HF ratio (r=-0.58, p<0.05). HRR1 was not correlated with HF and LF but was correlated with LF/HF (r=-0.49, p<0.05)

coNcLusIoNs: These data indicate that HRR after maximal arm exercise testing is associated with resting cardiac autonomic activity as measured with heart rate variability in persons with SC1. This would suggest that HRR after maximal arm exercise testing can be used for evaluation of autonomic function in this population.

# Effects of Resistance Exercise Training on Arterial Function in Young, Healthy Men and Women

Randy W. Braith, FACSM, Darren T. Beck, Darren P. Casey. University of Florida, Gainesville, FL. Email: rbraith@hhp.ufl.edu

PURPOSE: There is evidence that resistance exercise (RT) can increase central arterial stiffness during high-intensity and high-volume training regimens. The purpose of this study was to determine whether RT, when performed in accordance with ACSM guidelines, has deleterious effects upon central arterial function in young, healthy men and women. METHODS: Previously untrained subjects were assigned either to a group that performec RT 3 days/week for 12 weeks (TRAIN; n = 22; 19.8 ± 1.4 yrs) or to a control group that did not train (CoNTR; n = 12; 20.7 ± 2.9 yrs). Training consisted of 2 sets of 12 repetitions performed to volitional failure on 7 variable resistance machines. Resting aortic augmentation index normalized at 75 bpm (AIa), round trip travel time of aortic pressure waves (Dtp), and regional arterial pulse wave velocity (Pwv) were measured noninvasively using applanation tonometry. Regional Pwv (m/sec) between the carotid-radial (CRPWV), carotid-femoral (CFPWV), and femoral-dorsalis pedis (FDPWV) arteries were calculated by determining the delay between the appearance of the pressure waveform foot in each site with simultaneous ECG. AIa and Dtp were calculated from aortic pressure waveforms, which were synthesized using a generalized transfer function from high fidelity radial pressure waveforms calibrated from standard brachial artery sphygmomanometry in triplicate. RESULTS: AIa and Dtp were unchanged in TRAIN (from -1.6±14.1 to 1.2±10.5 %; from 157.4±28.3 to 155.5±12.8 msec) and CoNTR (from -4.0±9.1 to -3.4±10.2 %: from 151.3±11.6 to 154.7±10.6 msec). CRPWV, CFPWV, and FDPWV were unchanged in TRAIN (from 8.4±1.1 to 8.0±1.0 m/s; from 6.2±0.6 to 6.3±0.8 m/s; from 9.4±1.5 to 9.6±1.4 m/s) and CoNTR (from 8.2±0.7 to 8.0±0.6 m/s; from 7.0±0.7 to 6.9±0.8 m/s; from 8.3±1.1 to 8.1±1.1 m/s). TRAIN significantly (p≤0.05) increased Chest Press (+37.5%) and Leg Extension (+41.9%) strength. CöNTR values for Chest Press (+1.0%) and Leg Extension (+0.5%) strength did not change. CONCLUSION: RT had no adverse effects upon either amplitude and timing of aortic reflected pressure or regional arterial PWV in young, healthy men and women when training was performed consistent with ACSM guidelines.

# Mechanical Compression Reduces Regional Arterial Stiffness

Kevin S. Heffernan², David G. Edwards², Lindy Rossow1, Sae Young Jae1, Bo Fernhall, FACSM1. 'University of Illinois at Urbana Champaign, Champaign, IL. University of Delaware, Newark, DE. Email: kheffer2@uiuc.edu

puRposE: Acute aerobic and resistance exercise has been shown to reduce local muscular artery stiffness in the exercised limb while having no effect on the non-exercised limb. The stimulus for these modulations may be related to local muscular compression of underlying vasculature, The purpose of this study was to examine regional arterial stiffness before and after a series of locally applied mechanical compressions designed to mirror the resistance exercise concentric/eccentric duty cycle. METHoDs: Two rapidly inflatable cuffs were placed around the quadriceps and calf of the left leg in 14 healthy, young (22±2 yrs) participants (female n = 8). Cuffs were inflated to a supra-systolic pressure of 200 mmHa for 4 seconds followed by a 2 second rapid deflation period. One "set" consisted of 12 inflation/deflation cycles. 6 sets of 12 cycles of compression were performed. Pulse wave velocity (Pwv) was used to measure central stiffness (carotid to femoral) and peripheral stiffness (femoral to dorsalis pedis of both legs) before and 10 min after mechanical compressions. REsuLTS: No change was found in central PWV (6.0±0.3 m/s to 5.9±0.2 m/s, p>0.05). A leg-by-time interaction was found as peripheral PWV in the noncompressed leg did not change (8.6±0.4 m/s to 8.3±0.4. p>0.05) while peripheral Pwv in the compressed leg significantly decreased from pre to 10 min post (8.5±0.4 to 7.8±0.4, p<0.05). There were no changes in systolic blood pressure, diastolic blood pressure, and heart rate. coNcLusIoNs: Physical compression reduced local muscular artery stiffness of the compressed leg while having no effect on stiffness of the non-compressed contralateral leg or central elastic arteries. Reductions in regional stiffness seen with acute exercise may be related to mechanical effects of contraction.

# Effects of 8-Week Isometric Handgrip Training on Resting Arterial Pressure

Philip J. Millar, Neil McCartney, Maureen J. MacDonald. McMaster University, Hamilton, ON, Canada. Email: millarpj@mcmaster.ca

Hypertension (HTN) is the chronic elevation of resting blood pressure and is estimated to affect 30% of the global population. HTN is treated with lifestyle modifications and pharmacologic agents. Isometric handgrip training (IHG) has been shown to reduce resting blood pressure in normotensive and hypertensive individuals following training using programmable, digital handgrips. The purpose was to replicate previous IHG results with common spring handgrips. A randomized control trial of 49 normotensive participants (66.4±0.9 years) was conducted. Participants who were randomized to the exercise group (n=25) trained 3 times per week for 8 weeks. Control participants (n=24) completed weekly blood pressure measurements. Blood pressure was assessed during 3 pre and 3 post-training visits. Statistical analysis was completed using a two-way ANOvA. Following 8 weeks of IHG exercise trained participants demonstrated significant reductions in resting blood pressure and pulse pressure. Systolic and diastolic blood pressures were reduced from 122±3 mmHq to 112±3 mmHq (p<0.001) and 70±1 mmHq to 67±1 mmHg (p<0.05) respectively. Pulse pressure was reduced by 7 mmHg following IHG training (52±3 mmHg to 45±3 mmHg, p<0.05). Heart rate remained unaltered with training. No change in any measure over the study duration was observed for participants in the non-exercise control group. In agreement with previous studies, IHG reduced resting arterial pressure following 8 weeks of training. Specifically, readily available spring handgrips appear to be able to reproduce the results previously observed using programmable, digital handgrip devices Supported by the National Sciences and Engineering Research Council of Canada Discovery Grant (NSERC)

# Effects of Menstrual Cycle and Aerobic Fitness on Cardiorespiratory and Temperature Responses During Sinusoidal Exercise

Ryoko Sone1, Nobusuke Tan1, Fumio Yamazaki². 1Yamaguchi University, Yamaguchi-city, Japan. 2University of Occupational and Environmental Health, Kitakyusyu-city, Japan. Email: sone@yamaguchi-u.ac.jp

Objects. Menstrual cycle is a modulatory factor in the cardiorespiratory and thermoreaulatory responses to exercise in female subjects, We reported previously that dynamic responsiveness in heart rate (HR) and oxygen consumption (VO2) to sinusoidal exercise in which work rate varies sinusoidally from mild to moderate are heightened by endurance training in male subjects. However, whether the effect of endurance training on these responses differs between the phases of menstrual cycle remains unknown. Therefore, we examined the dynamic responsiveness in cardiorespiratory and temperature variables in response to sinusoidal exercise during the follicular and luteal phases in twc groups of women with different levels of aerobic fitness. Methods. The subjects were nine sedentary (S-group, age 21+/-1 yrs, height 157+/-6 cm, weight 54+/-8 kg, VO2peak 32+/-3 ml/kg/min) and nine endurance-trained women (T-group, age 20+/-1 yrs, height 159+/-3 cm, weight 53+/-2 kg, VO2peak 44+/-3 ml/kg/min). All subjects were healthy and eumenorrhoeic, and plasma progesterone concentrations did not significantly differ between the two groups during either menstrual phase. Using a cycle ergometer driven by computer, sinusoidal exercise test in which work rate varied sinusoidally between 10 and 60 %Vo2reserve for an 8-min period were obtained once during the follicular and luteal phases, respectively. HR, VO2, rate of perceived exertion (RPE), and esophageal temperature (Tes) were measured continuously during sinusoidal exercise, and the amplitude and time delay (TD) of the responses were analyzed. Results. The baseline Tes and HR were higher in the luteal compared with that during the follicular phase (P<0.05). A smaller HR amplitude (P<0.05) and the greater amplitude and shorter TD in Tes (P<0.15) occurred during in the luteal phase compared with those during the follicular phase Independently of the phases of menstrual cycle, greater amplitudes in HR and RPE (p<0.05) and shorter TDs in HR and VO2 (P<0.05) were observed in the T-group compared with the S-group. Conclusions. It was indicated that the amplitude responsiveness in HR and the capacity to maintain core body temperature during sinusoidal exercise were lowered in the luteal compared with those in the follicular phase. It was suggested that the dynamic responsiveness in HR, VO2, and RPE would be heightened by endurance training independently of the phases of menstrual cycle in healthy young women.

puRposE: The purpose of the present study was to assess the equivalency between the VO2 reserve (VO2R), heart rate reserve (HRR), and maximal heart rate (HRmax) methods to prescribe an exercise target heart rate (HR) following a maximal graded exercise test in obese individuals. METHoDs: Sixty-eight overweight to severely obese and sedentary adults (mean ± standard deviation) (BMI = 34.9±4.6 kg•m?, resting HR = 84±11 bpm resting VO2 = 2.5±0.5 ml•kg1, HRmax = 178±11 bpm, VO2peak = 25.1±4.3 ml kgmin-1 performed a Bruce ramp protocol on a treadmill. RESULTS: Target HR calculated by HRR was 6±7, 7±8, 7±8, 6±8, 5±7, and 4±6 bpm significantly higher (p<0.001) than VOR from I-1 to I-6 respectively. Target HR calculated by $H R _ { m a x }$ was not significantly different than VO>R at I-1 (-2±9) and I-2 (1±10) but was siqnificantly higher (p<0.01) from I-3 to I-6 and was equal to 3±9, 5±8, 8±7, and 7±7 bpm respectively. Target HR calculated by HRmax was -9±6, -6±5, and -4±4 bpm significantly lower (p<0.001) than HRR from I-1 to I-3, not significantly different at I-4 (-1±3), and significantly higher (p<0.001) at I-5 (2±2) and I-6 (3±2). No intensity effect for delta target HR between HRR and VO2R was found. Delta target HR between HRrax and VO2R were significantly different (p<0.001) from each other from I-1 to 1-5 but not between I-5 and I-6. Delta target HR between $\hat { \mathsf { H } } _ { \mathsf { m a x } }$ and HRR were significantly different from each other between intensities (p<0.001). coNcLusIoNs: The slightly but significant (6±7 bpm) difference between target HR calculated by HRR and VO2R is constant among intensities. However, the difference between target HR calculatec by HRmax and VO2R or HRmax and HRR both fluctuate between relative intensities. These results have their importance for exercise target HR prescription in obese individuals.

# The Effect of Training Status on Carotid Arterial Compliance in Peri-Pubertal Children

Megan F.B. Sherman1, Mark Rakobowchuk1, Carla L.M. Guerts², Jose F. Peralta-Huertas²2, Panagiotoa Klentrou², Maureen J. MacDonald². McMaster University, Hamilton, ON, Canada. 2Brock University, Hamilton, ON, Canada.

Aerobic exercise has been shown to improve total systemic arterial compliance in men (Cameron and Dart, Am, Physiol, Soc. 266:1994); however, this effect has yet to be observed in trained children. PuRposE: The purpose of our study was to investigate arterial compliance in sedentary peri-pubertal children, compared with trained peripubertal swimmers. METHoDs: V02peak, carotid artery compliance (combined ultrasound and applanation tonometry), maturity (Tanner Self-Assessment Scale), blood pressure, height, weight, and lean body mass (Ibm; skinfold thickness) were assessed in 14 trained girls (age: 10.5 ± 1.0y), and 7 trained boys (age: 10.6 ± 1.3y) and compared to 8 sedentary girls (age: 10.3 ± 0.96y) and 10 sedentary boys (age: 10.6 ± 1.0y). REsuLTS: Significant differences were found between the two groups for V02peak (61.3 ± 1.0 vs. 55.6 ± 2.4ml·kg Ibm"1-min; p<0.05), carotid pulse pressure (37.9 ± 1.0 vs. 50.6 ± 1.6mmHg; p<0.05) and BMI (18.0 ± 0.5 vs. 19.5 ± 0.5kg·m^2;p<0.05). However, crosssectional arterial compliance was not different between the two groups (0.18 ± 0.01 vs. 0.18 ± 0.01mm²·mmHg-1, p> 0.05). Correlational analyses were performed to assess possible relationships between all variables measured. No significant correlation existed between V0zpeak and cross-sectional arterial compliance. These results suggest that the relationship between training status, fitness level, and central arterial compliance that exist in adults may not be present prior to the completion of puberty. conclusIoN: Thus, maturity may have a greater impact on arterial stiffness than training status, as all the subjects we studied were in similar Tanner stages. Supported by NSERC and CIHR.

## Resistance Training and Central Artery Compliance: Keeping the Benefits While Minimizing the Stiffening

Motohiko Miyachi², Hiroshi Kawano², Hirofumi Tanaka, FACsM³. 1National Institute of Health and Nutrition, Tokyo, Japan, 2Waseda University, Tokyo, Japan, 3University of Texas, Austin, TX. Email: miyachi@nih.go.jp

PURposE: Resistance training has become a popular mode of exercise, but intense weight training is shown to stiffen central arteries. The aim of this study was to determine the effects of moderate resistance training as well as the combined resistance and aerobic training intervention on central arterial compliance.

METHoDs: Fifty-three young healthy men were assigned either to the high-intensity resistance training (HIGH), the moderate-intensity resistance training (MODE), the combined resistance training and endurance training (CoMBo) or the sedentary control (CoNTROL) groups. Subjects in the training groups underwent 3 training sessions/wk for 4 months followed by 4 additional months of detraining

REsuLTs: All training groups increased maximal strength in all the muscle groups tested (P<0.05). The HIGH and MODE groups experienced \~20% reductions in carotid arterial compliance after resistance training (HIGH: from 0.21+/-0.02 mm2/mmHg to 0.17+/-0.01 mm2/mmHg, P<0.01, MODE: from 0.20+/-0.01 mm2/mmHg to 0.16+/-0.01 mm2/mmHg P<0.01, Figure). No significant changes in carotid arterial compliance were observed in the COMBO (from 0.20+/-0.01 mm2/mmHg to 0.23+/-0.01 mm2/mmHg, P=0.06) and CONTROL (from 0.20+/-0.01 mm2/mmHg to 0.20+/-0.01 mm2/mmHg, NS) groups. Following the detraining period, carotid arterial compliance returned to the baseline level. Peripheral (femoral) artery compliance did not change in any groups.

coNcLusIoNs: Simultaneously-performed aerobic exercise training could prevent the stiffening of large elastic arteries caused by resistance training in young healthy men.

# Exercise Capacity and Duration Improves in Patients with End-Stage Renal Disease After Conversion to Nocturnal Hemodialysis

Catherine F. Notarius², Christopher T. Chan², Anthony Merlocco³, John S. Floras1 1University Health Network and Mount Sinai Hospital, University of Toronto, Toronto, ON Canada. 2University Health Network , University of Toronto, Toronto, ON, Canada. 3University Health Network, Toronto, ON, Canada. Email: c.notarius@utoronto.ca

Patients with end-stage renal disease (ESRD) have a reduced peak oxygen uptake (VO2peak) during incremental exercise as compared to healthy age-matched subjects. Nocturnal hemodialysis (NHD) [5 -6 sessions per week; 6 - 8 hours per session] augments uremic clearance and lowers blood pressure (BP) via a decrease in total peripheral resistance.

PURposE: To determine whether exercise capacity improves in ESRD patients, at 2 and 3 6 months following conversion to nocturnal hemodialysis.

METHoDS: We studied 12 consecutive ESRD patients (age: 42 ± 3 years; [mean ± SEM] before, and 2 and 3-6 months after conversion from conventional hemodialysis (CHD) [3 sessions per week; 4 hours per session] to NHD and 14 age and body mass index-matched sedentary healthy subjects (age:42 ± 3 years). Oxygen consumption was measured breath by breath using open-circuit spirometry during a graded bicycle ergometer test (17W/min) to peak effort. Heart rate, BP, exercise time, dialysis dose (Kt/V per treatment) and related dialysis biochemistry were determined for each patient study. Exercise capacity was expressed as a percentage of predicted VOzpeak accounting for age, sex, and body size. Total daily energy expenditure (TDEE) of patients was assessed by administration of the Stanford 7-Day Recall Physical Activity Questionaire.

REsuLTS: After conversion to NHD, there was an increase in dialysis dose and reductions in clinic BP, requirement for antihypertensive medications, plasma phosphate and parathyroid hormone (PTH) levels. Hemoqlobin, albumin, potassium and bicarbonate levels remained unaltered. There was a progressive increase in VO>peak (from 67 ± 8 [CHD] to 69 ± 6 [NHD 2 months] and 77 ± 7% predicted [NHD 3-6 months], p = 0.02) and exercise duration (from 618 ± 50 [CHD] to 618 ± 53 [NHD 2 months] and 702 ± 60 s [NHD 3 - 6 months] $\mathsf { \bar { p } } = \mathsf { 0 } . 0 1 8 ) _ { i }$ whereas normal subjects had a $\mathsf { V O } _ { 2 \mathsf { p e a k } }$ which was 90 ± 4 % of predicted (p=0.02 vs CHD) and an exercise duration of 722 ± 53 s(p<0.001 vs CHD). TDEE of patients remained constant

coNcLusIoNs:Exercise capacity significantly improves toward normal values following 3 6 months of conversion to nocturnal hemodialysis. We speculate that this could result from enhanced oxygen delivery through recruitment of capillary network and alterations in muscle metabolism via enhanced uremia clearance Supported by Canadian Institutes for Health Research.

# Effects of Prior Exercise on Insulin-stimulated AS160 Phosphorylation and Glucose Transport in Rat Skeletal Muscle

Katsuhiko Funai, Junghoon Kim, Edward B. Arias, Gregory D. Cartee, FACSM. University of Michigan, Ann Arbor, MI. Email: kfunai@umich.edu

A single exercise session can lead to a subsequent increase in insulin-stimulated glucose transport in skeletal muscle, secondary to enhanced recruitment of GLuT4 glucose transporter to the cell surface. Akt Substrate of 160 kDa (AS160), a protein recently identified to participate in insulin-stimulated GLUT4 translocation, is a potential contributor to the improved insulin action after exercise. PuRPosE: The purpose of this study was to determine if acute exercise leads to a subsequent change in AS160 abundance or phosphorylation in skeletal muscle, with or without physiologic insulin. METHoDs: Rats were randomly assigned to post-exercised (PEX; 2 hr swimming) or sedentary (SED) groups. At \~3-4 hr post-exercise, rats were anesthetized, and paired epitrochlearis muscles were dissected out. One muscle per rat was incubated without insulin, and the contralateral muscle was incubated with 50 µU/ml insulin. The rate of 3-O-[3H]methyl-D glucose transport (3MGT), protein phosphorylation [Ser-Akt phosphorylation (pSerAkt), Thr-Akt phosphorylation (pThrAkt), and AS160 phosphorylation (pAS160)], and AS160 abundance were determined. Data were analyzed by 2-way ANovA (Exercise x Insulin). Insulin-stimulated values (Δ-insulin) for 3MGT and protein phosphorylation were calculated as the values for insulin-treated muscles minus the values for contralateral controls incubated without insulin. PEX and SED Δ-insulin values were compared by a t-test. REsuLTS: As expected, insulin led to an increase in 3MGT, pSerAkt, pThrAkt, and pAS160, with or without exercise. There was a significant Main Effect of Exercise on 3MGT, pThrAkt and pAS160. Post-hoc analysis revealed that 3MGT and pThr-Akt were significantly greater for PEX vs. SED in muscles treated with insulin, but not without insulin. PEX tended to exceed SED for pAS160 in muscles without (P=0.063) or with (P=0.098) insulin despite no exercise effect on AS160 abundance. There was a significant Exercise-Insulin Interaction only for 3MGT. Δ-Insulin 3MGT was greater for PEX vs. SED, but Δ-Insulin pAS160 was unaltered by exercise. concLusIoN: The lack of a significant Exercise-Insulin Interaction for pAS160 or exercise effect on Δ-insulin pAS160 indicates that exercise did not amplify the ability of insulin to increase pAS160. Thus, an exercise-induced enhancement of insulin-stimulated pAS160 was not required for the increased insulin-stimulated alucose transport after exercise. However, these results do not eliminate the possibility that the exercise effect on pAS160, working with other processes that require insulin, may play a role in elevated insulin-stimulated glucose transport post-exercise

## Metabolic

# DNP-Stimulated GLUT4 Translocation via 5'-AMP Activated Protein Kinase is Dependent on Plasma Membrane Cholesterol and Potentially Coupled Phosphoinositide-Regulated Actin Polymerization Events

Kirk M. Habegger, Emily M. Horvath, Jeffrey S. Elmendorf. Indiana University School of Medicine, Indianapolis, IN.

Membrane and cytoskeletal mechanics have been suggested to play an important role in the regulation of GLuT4 translocation. Here we report that an aspect of exercise/contraction-mediated GLuT4 translocation may involve a favorable regulation of plasma membrane cholesterol by 5'-AMP-activated protein kinase (AMPK). Moreover, subsequent cholesterol-dependent alterations in plasma membrane PIP, and cortical F. actin may be coupled to the AMPK effect. In L6 myotubes stably expressing GLUT4 that carries an exofacial myc-epitope tag, insulin or 2,4-dinitrophenol (DNP) treatment increased GLUT4myc translocation by \~2-fold. In addition, the insulin response was amplified \~80% in the presence of DNP. We show that treatment of L6 myotubes with DNP led to a characteristic increase in AMPK phosphorylation at threonine 172. Consistent with AMPK inhibition of 3-hydroxy-3-methylglutaryl coenzyme A (HMG-CoA) reductase, a ratelimiting enzyme in cholesterol biosynthesis, we found DNP treatment promoted a significant 17% loss of plasma membrane cholesterol. Interestingly, cholesterol add-back to the plasma membrane prevented the beneficial effect of DNP on basal and the enhanced insulin-stimulated GLUT4 mobilization. With regard to cholesterol-laden membranes potentially contributing to impaired insulin responsiveness, we found that both insulin- and lipid-induced insulin-resistant states are associated with an \~40% increase in plasma membrane cholesterol, Interestinaly, a Iabeling experiment of cholesterol-enrichec membrane microdomains present in highly purified plasma membrane sheets reveals a clear PIP, colocalization, which seems to intensify in insulin-resistant cells. AMPk activation by trivalent chromium, which decreases plasma membrane cholesterol, appears associated with lateral movement of PIP2 out of the cholesterol-enriched microdomain. Together, with findings linking the depolymerization of F-actin to changes in plasma membrane PIP2, we find that both the hyperinsulinemic- and hyperlipidemic-states negatively affect F-actin structure. These data are consistent with the idea that excess circulatory insulin and FFAs may negatively impact insulin sensitivity by affecting plasma membrane phospholipid balance. We propose a beneficial component of exercise-induced GLuT4 regulation in skeletal muscle may involve inhibition of HMG-CoA reductase activity bv AMpk and correction of insulin- and lipid-induced insulin resistance involving altered cholesterol/PIP2- regulated F-actin polymerization.

# Influence of Tumor Promoting Prolactin, Energy Balance, and Gene Expressions on Spontaneous Tumor Development in Laboratory Animals

Helaine M. Alessio, FACSM, Natalie B. Schweitzer, Angela M. Snedden, Callahan Phyllis, Ann E. Hagerman. Miami University, Oxford, OH. Email: alessih@muohio.edu

Tumor growth is impacted by energy balance, a tumor-promoting hormone, prolactin, and genetics. Physical inactivity can affect energy imbalance and blood prolactin concentration, which could ultimately modify gene expression associated with tumor regulation. Spontaneous tumors occur in 45-71% of Sprague-Dawley (SD) laboratory rats, yet few studies have considered the potential impact of the inherent sedentary condition of a standard cage on tumorigenesis. Tumor profiles, microarray analyses, and tumoi promoting hormone prolactin were compared to determine how residing in a standard cage affects tumor development. Female SD rats were sorted into 1. cage with no outside activity (SED), 2. twice-weekly, one hour physical activity in large box (PA), and 3. regular voluntary running wheel exercise (EX) groups. SED had higher tumor number and size throughout most of their lifespan compared with EX. PA had tumor profiles similar to SED. Higher tumor burden (54%) in SED vs. EX (38%), although falling in the range of previous studies, may be abnormally high due to the sedentary setting of a standard cage. At 16 months, SED tumors included thyroid carcinoma, malignancy, mammary fibroadenoma, cystadenoma, and granuloma when compared with benign mammary gland cysts that were most common in EX. Eight cardiac genes related to tumors were down-regulated in SED vs. EX (i.e. Rab40c, Lyn, Rab5b, Mycn, Tnfsf13, Fyn Brca1, and LOC291530), and five were up-regulated (i.e. Tnfrsf4, C1qtnf1, Tnfsf9, Dlc1, and RGD:619831). Prolactin [evels were highest in SED at ages 6 and 12 months (p < 0.05), after which age-associated hyperprolactinemia occurred. In conclusion, when compared with animals that had access to exercise, animals housed in standard cages had a larger number and size of tumors that included malignancy, as well as higher prolactin levels, lower energy intake,lower energy expenditure,and thirteen different tumor-associated gene expressions.

Anjaiah Katta², Kevin M. Rice², Deborah L. Preston1, Sunil K. Kakarla1, Eli Yokochi1, J. Brandon Armstrong², Eric R. Blough³. 1Marshall University, Huntinqton, WV. 2Marshall University, and Joan C. Background: Muscle contraction alone is able to initiate glucose uptake via an insulir independent mechanism. Whether differences exist between normal and diabetic muscle in the activation of these molecules following an acute bout of resistance exercise remains unclear. Methods: plantaris and soleus from lean (nondiabetic) and obese (diabetic) Zucker rats were isolated following during the recovery phase of an acute bout of high-frequency electrical stimulation. Conclusion: The results suggest that the intramuscular signaling processes are regulated differently in diabetic muscle compared to normal. (Supported by funds from NSF Grant #0314742
