## Cardiovascular

# Cardiac Adaptation to Heat Acclimation and Exercise Training: Molecular-Physiological Linkage in Force Generation

Einat Kodesh, Nir Nesher, Michal Horowitz. Hebrew university of jerusalem, Jerusalem, Israel. Email: kodesh@nob.huii.ac.il

PuRposE: Heat acclimation and exercise training induce adaptive changes in the heart such as decreased resting heart rate, elevated stroke volume and metabolic efficiency. Given the unigue metabolic demands of each stressor, these adaptations are achieved differently. Heat acclimation facilitates increased cardiac pressure at a decreased metabolic rate and enhances compliance, whereas the trained heart exhibits hypertrophy. In this study we characterized the emerging physiological and molecular adaptations to these stressors.

METHoDs: Male rats, Rattus norvegicus were divided into 4 groups. Sedentary: Normothermic Controls - 24° C (CO) and heat acclimated - 34° C for 30 days (AC). Trained: normothermic (EX) and Acclimated (EXAC) rats were subjected to an increasing exercise load on a treadmill (0.64 m/sec), 1 hr/d, 5 d/week, for 1 month, A cDNA Atlas Array (Clontech Rat 1.2 array) was used to screen the genomic changes. Inward calcium currents (ICa) and action potentials in isolated ventricular cardiomyocytes were determined using a whole cell recording technigue. Ca2+ transients and contractile force generation in single cells were measured using a Fluorometic apparatus and Edge detector systems, respectively.

RESULTS: Among the 800 genes detected, more were upregulated. The EXAC group expressed the smallest number of genes. Genes encoding sarcoplasmic Ca² regulatory proteins, L-type Ca2+ channel as well as those associated with K+ currents were profoundly upregulated in the heat acclimated hearts. Pathways associated with Ca 2+ regulatory proteins, Ca²\* signaling and muscle contraction were upregulated in all the groups (albeit differently in each group). The expression of the Ca²+ signaling pathways predominated in AC, whereas the expression of the Ca2+ regulatory genes was the highest in EXAC. Previous findings of enhanced contractile force in AC and EX together with the variations in genomic responses among the groups, caused us to focus on the contractile properties of the myocytes. ICa were higher in CON and EX than in the AC and EXAC groups. Trained groups generated higher ICa amplitudes vs. sedentary. Concomitantly, a higher Ca2+ transient was needed to generate similar contractile forces in groups exposed to heat, suggesting that the myofilaments became less sensitive to Ca²+.

coNcLusroNs: There is causal evidence for a link between the studied physiological and genomic responses. Therefore different molecular adaptations were used to produce the similar physiological phenotype of increased cytosolic Ca²† concentration.

# In Vivo Assessment of Resting Myocardial Function in High and Low Performing Genetic Models of Aerobic Running Capacity

Eric E. Morgan, Samer J. Khouri, Ann Anderson, Ramona Ramdath, Justin A. Ways, George T. Cicila. University of Toledo College of Medicine. Toledo. OH Email: emorgan@meduohio.edu

BACKGRouND: We previously identified two inbred rat strains divergent for treadmill aerobic running capacity (ARC); the low performing Buffalo (BUF) and the high performing DA rats. Subsequent cardiac performance assessment using the Langendorff-Neely working heart preparation demonstrated that isolated DA hearts exhibit increased stroke volume and cardiac output compared to the BuF hearts, with no difference in intrinsic heart rate (HR). In the present study, we investigated in vivo resting cardiac function in BUF and DA rats using echocardiography.

METHoDS: Male DA (n=11) and BUF (n=12) rats (aged 14-17 weeks) were lightly anesthetized with pentobarbital (40mg/kg, ip), the chest was shaved, and the animal was situated in the supine position on a warming pad. Two-dimensional guided M-mode studies were performed from the parasternal long axis for the determination of left ventricular end diastolic (LVDd) and end-systolic (LVSd) diameters and fractional shortening (FS). The myocardial performance index (MPI), stroke volume (SV) and cardiac output (Co) were calculated using Doppler pulsed-wave traces of mitral and aortic flow measured at the level of the LV outflow tract from the apical four-chamber view. Cardiac index (CI) was calculated by dividing Co by body weight

RESULTS: BUF rats had significantly greater body weight and a larger SV than the DA rats but had significantly lower HR and CI (Table). No differences in LVDd, LVSd, FS, MPI, or CO were observed (Table)

coNcLUsIoN: After correcting for differences in HR and body weight, in vivo assessment of resting cardiac function revealed that DA rats exhibit greater cardiac performance than BUF rats, Our findings contrast with those of the working heart preparation in that in vivo assessment of cardiac function shows DA rats to have a smaller stroke volume and greater HR than the BUF rats, resulting in an identical CO between the two strains. Nevertheless cardiac index is significantly greater in DA rats, possibly contributing to the superior ARC observed in this rat strain compared to that of the BUF strain

## Altered Dynamic Heart Rate Control in M2-Muscarinic Acetylcholine Receptor Knockout Mice

Hirotsuqu Tsuchimochi², James T. Pearson², Minoru Matsui², Daryl O. Schwenke², Hidezo Mori, Mikiyasu Shirai. "National Cardiovascular Center Research Institute, Osaka, Japan. Medical Science, University of Tokyo, Tokyo, Japan. ³Faculty of Health Sciences, Hiroshima International University, Higashi Hiroshima, Japan. Email: omochi@hiroshima-u.ac.jp

PURPosE: It is thought that the initial increase in heart rate (HR) at the onset of dynamic exercise is caused by rapid withdrawal of cardiac parasympathetic tone, but not by an increase in sympathetic nerve activity. The M receptor (of M-Ms muscarinic receptors) is believed to be predominantly expressed in cardiac muscle. Therefore, we examined the role of M2 receptor in HR control during voluntary wheel running exercise using M2-KO mice.

METHopS: Male M>-KO mice and age-matched wild-type (WT) control animals, all between 10 and 15 wk of age and weighing 22-29 g, were used. For ambulatory long-term electrocardiogram (EcG) analysis in the conscious state, telemetry devices (model TA10- F20; DataSciences International, St. Paul, MN) were implanted with the use of a sterile technique. The transmitter was implanted in the peritoneal cavity of each mouse at least 7 days before start of the experiments. The electrodes were seated subcutaneously, the (-) lead was positioned and sutured subcutaneously at the right shoulder, and the (+) lead was sutured towards the lower left chest. HR was determined by electrocardiogram telemetry in conscious mice.

RESULTS: M2-KO mice showed slight tachycardia and more diminished respiratory oscillations in HR than WT mice at rest. Muscarinic agonist bethanechol induced marked vagal bradycardia and respiratory oscillations in HR in WT, but not in Mz-KO mice. The pressor challenge was performed after β-adrenergic blockade so that any observed change in HR would be due to activation of the inhibitory limb of the baroreflex and not the result of sympathetic withdrawal. Phenylephrine-induced hypertension elicited less bradycardia in M2-KO than in WT mice. With the onset of exercise, HR increased rapidly and remained elevated throughout exercise. This initial HR increase was blunted by a β-adrenergic blocker propranolol in both groups. A gradual increase in respiratory oscillation of HR observed in WT was absent in Mz-KO mice after exercise cessation. A delaved decrease in HR from the elevated level immediately after exercise suggests that the parasympathetic component plays an important role in HR recovery after dynamic exercise.

coNcLusroNs: These results indicate that vagal bradycardia and the respiratory sinus arrhythmia in the inactive state are chiefly mediated by the M, receptor, whereas cardiac acceleration at the onset and during dynamic exercise is not solely mediated by it. In conclusion, the parasympathetic component plays a more important role in lowering HR from activated levels.

# Uniaxial Stretch-Induced Regulation of Mitogen Activated Protein Kinase (MAPk) phosphorylation in Aging Rat Aorta

Kevin M. Rice1, Deborah L. Preston², Paulette Wehner³, Eric R. Blough1. 1Marshal/ University, and Joan C. Edwards School of Medicine, Marshall University, Huntington, WV. 2Marshall University, Huntington, WV. 3Joan C. Edwards Schoo! of Medicine, Marshall University, Huntington, WV. Email: rice9@marshall.edu

BAckGRouND: The effects of aging on the cardiovascular system contribute to substantial alterations in cellular morphology and function. The variables regulating these changes are unknown; however, one set of signaling molecules which may be of particular importance in mediating numerous cellular responses including control of cell growth, differentiation and adaptation are the proteins associated with the mitogen activated protein kinase (MAPK) signaling systems. Further MAPKs have emerged as critical components for regulating numerous mechanotrasductive cellular responses. Previous reports have suggested that agng impairs biaxial-loading induced MAPk phosphorylation How agigng may affect uniaxial mechanotrasductive processes is not clear.

PuRPOsE: Here we investigate the ability of a uniaxial stretch activate MAPK pathways in adult (6 mo old), aged (30 mo old) and very aged (36 mo old) Fischer 344 x Brown Norway rats.

METHoDs: Aortea of adult (6 month), aged (30 month), and very aged (36 month) Fischer 344/NNiaHSD X Brown Norway / BiNia rats were subjected to acute bout of a 20% uniaxial stretch. MAPk protein expression, basal phosphorylation and the uniaxial stretch induced changes in phosphorylation were evaluated by immunoblotting.

RESuLTS: Western blotting of the MAPK family proteins extracellular signal-requlated kinase (ERK) 1/2, p38-, and c-Jun NH2 -terminal kinase (JNK)-MAPKs showed differential expression and basal activation between these proteins with age, with notably higher phosphorylation in ERK1/2 and JNK compared to the 6 month aniumals. However, an acute bout of a 20% uniaxial stretch using an ex vivo aortic preparation demonstrated similar regulation of ERK 1/2 MAPK, p38-, and JNK MAPK.

coNCLusIoNs: These observations confirm previous data demonstrating MAPK proteins are mechanically regulated, and in addition, suggest that MApk sianaling following uniaxia stretch is not altered with aging. Taken together, these data may help explain the age related changes in vascular morphology, function and response to injury. (Supported by funds from NSF Grant #0314742)

# Resveratrol Affects Stretch-Induced Reorientation of A7r5 Cells in Culture

Rahamthulla S. Shaik², Kevin M. Rice², Devashish H. Desai³, Eric R. Blough, Robert T. Harris1. 1West Virginia State University, Institute, WV. 2Marshall University, and Joan C. Edwards School of Medicine, Marshall University, Huntington, WV. 3Joan C. Edwards School of Medicine, Marshall University, Huntington, WV. 4Marshall University, and Joan C. Edwards School of Medicine, Marshall Unviersity, Huntington, WV.

BAckGRouND: Excessive hemodynamic forces play a key role in vascular remodeling in hypertension and the origin of atherosclerosis in vivo. Smooth muscle is mostly restricted to its contractile phenotype in the adult but undergoes remarkable phenotypic modulation in response to various biomechanical stimuli. Mechanical strain induces changes in cell shape and the organization of the actin cytoskeleton. Members of the Rho sub family of small G proteins play important roles as modulators of both actin-based cytoskeletal reorganization and gene expression. Among the various responses of cells to stretch precise reorientation both in vivo and in vitro is one of the significant phenomena. However, the signalling mechanisms underlying the cytoskeletal reorganization and vascular smooth muscle orientation, particularly the role of small G proteins is poorly understood. Resveratrol (RV), phenolic natural component produced in grapes and a variety of other plants, has been thought to be responsible for cardiovascular benefits associated with moderate wine consumption but the mechanism of action of RV is not clear.

METHoDs: Smooth muscle cells of the A7r5 line were grown on specially designed six-wel tissue culture plates with type I collagen-coated flexible silicone bottoms. Quiescent, serum-starved (48 hr) cells were preincubated at different concentration (50-400uM) of RV or vehicle only (DMso) for 2 hr and then subjected to 5min, 15 min, 30 min, 1hr, 6 hr and 24 hr of uniaxial cyclic (0.5Hz) stretch, using a computer-controlled vacuum stretch apparatus (Fx-4oooT Tension Plus System) with a vacuum pressure that was sufficient to generate up to 15% mechanical strain. The cells were stained with Hematoxylin and Eosin and observed for stretch-induced reorientation morphology.

REsuLTS: The results of this study indicate that treatment of A7r5 cells with low concentrations of RV (< 100μM) resulted in a loss of their ability to respond precisely to high levels of mechanical stretch while, at higher concentrations of RV (>300μM), cells lost their spindle shape and failed to respond even in the presence of low levels of stretch.

coNcLusIoN: These results suggest that RV affects the reorientation response of A7r5 cells to stretch and appears to be G-protein mediated. (Supported in part by NIH grant RR16477 and NSF Grant 0314742)

17

# Strain differences in b-Endorphins in a Rat Genetic Models of Aerobic Running Capacity

Soon J. Lee, Ramona Ramdath, Justin A. Ways, Nicolas L. Chiaia, Krista Pettee, Sarah DeRaedt, George T. Cicila, University of Toledo, Toledo, OH Email: silee@meduohio.edu

PuRPosE: Aerobic running capacity (ARC) is a complex quantitative trait, determined by multiple genetic and environmental factors. Many physiological changes occur during exercise, including levels of endogenous opioids, specifically b-endorphins that can act as neurohormones and neurotransmitters throughout the peripheral and central nervous systems. Endorphins have many effects, including "exercise-induced euphoria," positive mood changes, modified pain perception and metabolism.

METHoDs: The impact of beta-endorphins in determining ARC was studied in a rat genetic model. Beta-endorphin levels in plasma and brain regions were studied with ELISA assays using antibodies specific for beta-endorphin in 11 week-old male rats from three inbred strains [DA, a hiqh performing strain and Copenhagen (COP) and Buffalo (BUF), low performing strains]. Experimental rat groups were studied following a single treadmill run to exhaustion and compared with control rats, which were not run. Two-way ANovA was used to identify significant differences in beta-endorphin levels, with P<0.05 the criterion for significance

REsuLTS: Basal plasma beta-endorphin levels were significantly different among the three strains [COP (n=19, 1.46±0.22 ng/ml), BUF (n=12, 5.52±0.54 ng/ml), and DA (n=14, 2.68±0.56 rats: P<0.0oo01). A Games-Howell post hoc test found sianificant differences between DA and BUF and between BUF and COP rats. Significantly increased plasma betaendorphin levels were also observed between rats after the single treadmill run compared to control rats (n=18, 3.56±0.62 ng/ml and n=27, 2.50±0.39 ng/ml, respectively; P=0.0oo8), but there was no significant interaction between these two factors. A significant difference in beta-endorphin levels in the periaquaductal area of the brain was observed among the three strains (P= 0.032). A PLSD post hoc test indicated a significant difference in periaquaductal beta-endorphin between COP (1.69±0.36 ng/ml) and BUF (2.87±0.31 ng/ml) rats (P=0.014). No significant differences in beta-endorphin levels were observed in the remaining brain regions examined: (striatum, thalamus, and hypothalamus) studied in these rats.

sIGNIFICANCE: This study indicates that the observed strain-differences in beta endorphin levels may contribute, in part, to the strain-differences in ARC seen in this model and that these inbred rat strains constitute a suitable genetic substrate for studying the basis for heritable strain differences in beta-endorphin levels.

# Identification of hemostatic genes expressed in human and rat leg muscles and a novel gene suppressed by sitting (LPP1/PAP2A)

Theodore W. Zderic, Lionel Bey, Marc T. Hamilton. University of Missouri-Columbia Columbia, MO.

Email: zderict@missouri.edu

A major paradigm shift largely because of functional genomics has been that tissues like muscle, heart, and adipose tissue are multifunctional, and can have paracrine or endocrine functions. Physical inactivity is an established risk factor for some blood clotting disorders. The effects of inactivity are most alarming when a person develops the eniamatic condition in the legs called deep venous thrombosis (DVT) or "coach syndrome," caused in part by muscular inactivity.

PuRposE: This study addressed the need to identify with microarrays, hemostasis genes expressed in leg skeletal muscle tissue, and with a translational approach, a novel candidate gene(s) that is most sensitive to physical inactivity

METHoDs: Global gene expression was assessed in deep leg muscle of humans using Affymetrix microarrays after 12 hours of sitting and after normal low-intensity standing and intermittent ambulatory activity. Global gene expression of the rat soleus muscle was also assessed with microarrays after 12 hours of physical inactivity (e.g., not standing), after 12 hours of physical inactivity followed by 2 hours of walking on a treadrill, after 11 days of intermittent physical inactivity (10 hr/day) and compared to the referent control of normally caged rats with voluntary standing and intermittent cage movements.

REsuLTs: These studies demonstrate that human skeletal muscle tissue expresses at least 22 genes involved in hemostasis. These include the fibrinolytic factors tetranectin, annexin A2, and tPA; the anti-coaqulant factors TFPI, protein C receptor, PAF acetylhydrolase; coagulation factors, and genes necessary for the posttranslational modification of these coagulation factors such as vitamin K epoxide reductase. Of special interest, lipid phosphate phosphatase-1 (LPP1/PAP2a), a key gene for degrading prothrombotic and proinflammatory lysophospholipids, was suppressed locally within muscle tissue within hours after sitting; this was also observed after acute and chronic physical inactivity conditions in rats, and exercise was relatively ineffective at counteracting this effect

coNcLusIoNs: These findings suggest that muscular inactivity contributes to hemostatic disorders not only because of the slowing of blood flow per se, but also potentially because of the contribution from genes expressed locally in muscles, such as LPP1.

# Rats Selectively Bred for High and Low Capacity Running (HCR/LCR) Phenotype do not Differ in Injury from Acute Myocardial Ischemia-Reperfusion.

Elizabeth Fontenot², Steven Britton², Lauren Koch², Robert Lust. 'East Carolina University, Greenville, NC. 2University of Michigan, Ann Arbor, MI. Email: egf0322@ecu.edu

PURposE: Forced artificial selection for intrinsic exercise capacity phenotypes have been developed (high-capacity, HCR and low-capacity runners, LCR) and previous reports have suggested that the HCR phenotype protects against pro-diabetic metabolic challenges. Exercise also has been associated with protection against cardiovascular diseases, therefore these studies were designed to determine whether HCR/LCR phenotype differences would be evident in responses to acute myocardial ischemia-reperfusion injury measured as infarct size.

METHops; Mvocardial ischemia-reperfusion was induced in intubated, mechanically ventilated anesthetized rats by a 30 min ligation of the left anterior descending coronary artery, followed by 2 hr of reperfusion. The infarct size was determined by 2, 3, 5- triphenyltetrazolium chloride staining, and normalized to area at risk

REsuLTS: Infarct size was not significantly different between the two groups (HCR, n=6, 43.9 ± 10.6, LCR, n=7, 42.5 ± 7.3 (mean ± SD), p= n.S.). Infarct size could not be correlated significantly with either body weight or run distance at selection.

CoNCLUsIoNs: These results suggest that in these models of endurance exercse capacity, the potential to exercise, in itself, does not alter myocardial injury induced by ischemia-reperfusion injury. This may suggest that active exercise might be more important in cardioprotection than the simple capacity to exercise.

## Measurement of Popliteal Venous Compliance with an Ultrasonography

Akira Miura. Aki Tsumura. Misuzu Ide. Makiko Urabe. Hatsumi Ueoka. Masako Yamaoka Endo, Yoshiyuki Fukuba, FACSM. Hiroshima Prefectural University, Hiroshima, Japan. Email: miura@pu-hiroshima.ac.jp

puRposE: An evaluation of the compliance of the whole venous system (including venule o arge vein) in human calf was developed with a venous congested plethysmography (VCpG) (Halliwil) et al., J.Appl.Physiol. 1999) which has already been used in several application studies. In general, the compliance in conduit arterial vessels (e.g., carotid artery for arteriosclerotic diagnosis) has evaluated from the change of vessel diameter (consequently the cross sectional area; CSA) which is induced by systolic-diastolic blood pressure (BP) fluctuation using B-mode ultrasonography (US). Utilizing the same strategy, we have proposed a similar technique for estimating the compliance in medium venous vessels (i.e., Popliteal vein) using US images, which is derived from the CSA-venous pressure (Pv) relationship. The purpose of this study was, therefore, to compare the venous compliances (Cv) estimated with our method in Popliteal vein and the previously proposed method with a VCPG in the calf, and to examine whether an activated sympathetic outflow to the vasculature decreases limb venous compliances.

METHoDs: Twelve healthy adult subjects (n supine position) were measured simultaneously via both methods (i.e., volume change by VCPG and vessel diameter change by US) during a continuously decreasing cuff pressure of right thigh (compatible to lower limb Pv) at the rate of 1mmHg/s for over 1 min following the 4 min constant inflation at 60 mmHg. Both compliances were calculated from the relationship between volume (Cvv) and CSA (Cvc) versus Pv over the range of pressures.

REsuLTs: The calf volume showed a gradual increase in all subjects even at the end of 4- min cuf inflation at 60 mmHg, whereas the diameter of the Popliteal vein reached a steady state within 1 min. The Cvc had a significant positive correlation to Cvv. In addition, Cvc and Cvv were also measured under the sympathoexcitation induced by the ischemic handgrip exercise to fatigue followed postexercise ischemia. Compared to the control, the calf volume was consistently smaller under the sympathoexcitation. The Cvc at 20mmHg of Pv was significantly lower.

coNcLustoNs: It is recommended that, in addition to the previously proposed method with VCPG, our proposed technique with US focusing on medium vein should be applied simultaneously. Supported in part by grant-in-Aid for Scientific Research from The Ministry of Education, Culture, Sports, Science and Technology in Japan (#185oo554)

## Cardiovascular

## The Effect of 52 Days Bed Rest on the Popliteal Venous Compliance in Healthy Subjects

Noortie T.L. van Duijnhoven, Patricia C.E. de Groot, Michiel W.P. Bleeker. Dick H.J. Thijssen, Maria T.E. Hopman, FACSM. Radboud University Nijmegen Medical Centre, Nijmegen, The Netherlands.

Email: n.vanduijnhoven@fysiol.umcn.

PuRposE: Physical inactivity is an independent risk factor for cardiovascular disease. Previous studies on the effects of deconditioning on leg venous compliance demonstrate conflicting results, reporting an increase, a decrease or no change. While these studies examined leg venous compliance, differential effects of deconditioning may be present looking at individual conduit veins. Recently, we introduced ultrasound as a suitable and reproducible method to examine compliance of a major conduit vein as the popliteal vein. The purpose of this study was therefore to assess the effect of bed rest on venous compliance of the popliteal vein measured with ultrasound. In addition, the effectiveness of resistive vibration exercise as a countermeasure for deconditioning during bed rest was evaluated.

METHoDS: Sixteen healthy men were randomly assigned to bed rest (BR-Ctrl) or to bed rest with resistive vibration exercise (BR-RVE). Before and after 52 days of strict horizonta bed rest, the diameter of the popliteal vein was measured by echo Doppler ultrasound at cuff pressures of 0, 20, 40, 60 and 80 mmHg. Venous compliance was calculated as the cross-sectional area of the popliteal vein at the different venous pressures.

REsuLTs: Overall, the diameter of the popliteal vein decreased significantiy after 52 days of bed rest at cuff pressures of 40, 60 and 80 mmHg (p≤ 0.01). The BR-Ctrl and the BR-RVE group showed a similar decrease in diameter, Popliteal venous compliance was unaltered after 52 days of bed rest in both the BR-Ctrl and BR-RVE group, with the exception of a decreased compliance at 80 mmHg in the BR-RVE group (p<0.05). No differences in venous compliance were present between the BR-Ctri and the BR-RVE group

coNcLustoNs: This study demonstrates that bed rest deconditioning is accompanied by a reduction in diameter of the popliteal vein. Assessing the venous compliance of this major conduit vein, venous function remains unaltered after 52 days of bed rest. Finally, resistive vibration exercise has no effect on both dimension and function of the venous vascular leg system.

## Cardiovascular

## Effects Of Exercise Training Amount And Intensity On Metabolic Syndrome: STRRIDE, A Randomized, Controlled Study

Cris A. Slentz, FACSM², Johanna L. Johnson², Joseph A. Houmard, FACSM², Brian D. Duscha, Lori B. Aiken, Jennifer S. McCartney², Gregory P. Samsa, William E. Kraus, FACSM1. 'Duke Univ., Durham, NC. 'East Carolina Univ., Greenville, NC. Email: cris.slentz@duke.edu

Exercise is beneficial for the individual risk factors of metabolic syndrome (MetSyn) defined by revised ATP III criteria. However. as the clinical definition of MetSyn is relatively new. there is little research on the effect of exercise on the syndrome as a whole, specifically the effects of different amounts and intensities of exercise.

METHODS: Of 387 subjects recruited for STRRIDE, 199 (93 women, 106 men) finished, had data for at least four of five risk factors, and were included in this analysis. Subjects were randomly assigned to six-month control or one of three eight-month exercise training groups: 1) high-amount/vigorous-intensity (calorically equal to 20 miles jogging/wk); 2) low-amount/vigorous-intensity (equal to 12 miles jogging/wk); 3) low-amount/moderateintensity (equal to 12 miles walking/wk).

REsuLTs: Analysis of variance shows a significant beneficial effect of exercise on MetSyn compared to the inactive control group. as the effects of all exercise groups were significantly better than controls (see figure). To determine if this difference was due to the detrimental effects of sedentary living, or to the ability of exercise to improve MetSyn, or both, paired t-tests on the individual effects of each group were performed (see figure). Metabolic syndrome scores deteriorated in controls (P=0.08). The lower amount of exercise at the moderate, but not vigorous intensity, significantly improved MetSyn score, suggesting that lower intensity exercise may be more effective than higher intensity exercise. The higher amount of vigorous intensity exercise also significantly improved MetSyn, indicating a dose-response relationship for vigorous intensity exercise. That is, some vigorous exercise is better than none, more vigorous exercise is better than less.

CoNcLUsoN: MetSyn score in the control group worsened, although this effect was just n he border of statistical significance. Exercise, compared to sedentary living, beneficially

affected MetSyn. The data, taken as a whole, suggest that lower intensity exercise may be more effective than more vigorous exercise and lends support to the 1995 CDC/ACSM recommendation that adults get 30 min of moderate intensity exercise most every day. The data also support a dose-response relationship for vigorous exercise, indicating that while some exercise is beneficial, more exercise (i.e. more kcals expended per week) was better.

# The Influence of Physical Activity Level on Monocyte Subpopulations

Kyle L. Timmerman, Michael G. Flynn, FACSM, Paul M. Coen, Melissa M, Markofski, Brandt Pence, Nicholas Woodall, Purdue Uoiversity, West Lafavette, IN Email: timmermk@purdue.edu

PuRposE: Physical activity has been reported to attenuate the age-related increase in markers of inflammation and to decrease the risk of developing age- and inflammatory: related diseases. The mechanisms underlying the anti-inflammatory effects of physical activity are not completely understood, but may involve physical activity-induced changes to the ratio of inflammatory vs. classical monocytes. Inflammatory monocytes are elevated in patients with sepsis and diseases linked to inflammation, and they are known to be potent producers of inflammatory cytokines. The purpose of the present study was to determine if habitual physical activity level and aerobic fitness are related to the percentage of circulating inflammatory monocytes. METHoDs: Male and female subjects, 65-85 yrs. old, were assigned to either a physically active group (PA, N=13) or a physically inactive group (P1, N=13) based on the Paffenbarger physical activity questionnaire and estimated VO2max, as determined by a modified Balke submaximal exercise test. Venous blood samples were collected and stained with conjugated antibodies specific for CD14 and CD16. A primary gate was established for the monocyte population based on forward and side liqht scatter. Inflammatory (CD14+CD16+) and classical monocyte (CD14++CD16-) subpopulations were quantified from CD14 and cD16 expression within the primary gate.

REsULTS: PA subjects had a significantly higher estimated VOmax than PI subjects (34.5 ml/kg/min vs. 20.2 ml/kg/min, p<0.05). The percentage of inflammatory monocytes was siqnificantly higher (p<0.05) for PI subjects (19.3%) than PA subjects (12.3%) Furthermore, there was a negative correlation between VOmax score and inflammatory monocyte percentage (r = -0.31, p= 0.068). coNcLusIoNs: To our knowledge, this is the first study to examine the relationship between physical activity/fitness level and the percentage of inflammatory monocytes. These results suggest that physically active 65-80 yr. olds have a lower percentage of inflammatory monocytes than physically inactive 65-80 yr. olds. This relationship may partially explain the anti-inflammatory effects of physical activity.

## Cardiovascular

# A Re-Appraisal of Cardiovascular Responses to Static Exercise

Bo Fernhall, FACSMk, Kevin S, Heffernan1, Sae Young Jae², George Echols², Gary Krull1 Thomas Rowland, FACSM². 1University of Illinois, Champaign, IL. 2Baystate Medical Center Springfield, MA. Email: fernhall@uiuc.edu

The cardiovascular responses to static exercise are characterized by a substantial increase in mean arterial pressure (MAP), mediated primarily by increased cardiac output (Q) as a function of increased heart rate (HR), with little change in stroke volume (SV) or periphera resistance (PR). However, pacing studies indicate isolated tachycardia simply decreases Sv without changing Q, and the high intramuscular pressure created by static exercise should increase PR. Thus, the current model does not adeguately explain the cardiovascular responses to static exercise, whereas responses to endurance exercise, substantial increase in HR, SV and Q, small increase in MAP and a decrease in PR, are adequately explained by the current model. We propose a new model, evaluating the responses per beat instead of per minute, and compared the responses to acute aerobic and static exercise using both the traditional and proposed new model. Fourteen children (age=10.1 ± 1.6 yrs) volunteered for the study and were tested using a cycle ergometry protocol (25W stages) and a 3 min static leg extension at 30% of MvC, in randomized order. We measured HR and MAP at rest and during exercise. Cardiac output and cardiac function were measured using conventional and Tissue Doppler ultrasound, both at rest and during exercise. PR was calculated from measured MAp and O values. Per beat flow was calculated from SV divided by systolic ejection time, and per beat PR was calculated from MAP/per beat flow. Results are shown in the table. Both the traditional and proposed per beat mode showed significant (p<.05) increases in HR, SV, Q and MAP, and a significant (p<.05) decrease in PR with aerobic exercise. However, during static exercise, the traditional mode showed a small decrease in SV, small increase in Q (p<.05), with no change in PR. The new model showed no change in per beat flow, but a significant increase in PR. This was facilitated by a decrease in systolic ejection time and an increase in systolic function (evaluated by Tissue Doppler, data not shown). Thus, we suggest that evaluating cardiovascular dynamics per beat, rather than per minute, provides a more intuitively attractive model which conforms better to expected hemodynamic events during both endurance and static exercise. The new model did not alter the interpretation of the

<table><tr><td>Type of Exercise</td><td>HER (cpm)</td><td>MAP(mmHg)</td><td>Q (Ucm)</td><td>Per beat flow (m/s)</td><td>PE conditional</td><td>PE per beat</td></tr><tr><td>REST</td><td>.89</td><td>.85</td><td>5.1</td><td>.222</td><td>.7</td><td>0.36</td></tr><tr><td>Static Exercise (30%)</td><td>.06</td><td>.09</td><td>5.6</td><td>.217</td><td>.9.6</td><td>0.51</td></tr><tr><td>Endurance Exercise</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>25W</td><td>.22</td><td>.88</td><td>8.5</td><td>.301</td><td>.70.5</td><td>0.30</td></tr><tr><td>50W</td><td>.41</td><td>.88</td><td>9.9</td><td>.321</td><td>.9.1</td><td>0.27</td></tr><tr><td>75W</td><td>.67</td><td>.91</td><td>11.6</td><td>.358</td><td>.7.8</td><td>0.24</td></tr><tr><td>100W</td><td>.93</td><td>.93</td><td>13.7</td><td>.416</td><td>.8.5</td><td>0.22</td></tr></table>

hemodynamic response to endurance exercise but showed that an abrupt rise in PR serves as a fundamental response to static exercise.

## Sport Activity in Athletes with MVP: What is the Impact ?

Marco Gianassi, M.C.R Vono, A Moretti, Laura Stefani, Valentina Di Tante, Manetti Paolo, Loira Toncelli, Giorgio Galanti. Azienda Ospedaliera Careggi-Medicine Sport centre-, Florence, Italy.

PURPosE: Mitral valve prolapse ( MPV) is the most common cardiac valve disorder in young people particularly in young women athletes (2-5% and 21% respectively ) The prognosis of MpV is generally favourable , but acute complications or chronic progression of the disease could be observed. Ecocardiography has markedly improved recognition and assessment of MpV and echo stress particularly plays an inportant role to predict exerciseinduced events. Aim of the study was to evaluate in a group of subjects at beginning of sport activity, mitral regurgitation (MR) , pulmonary artery pressure (PAP) and possible future modification in a follow-up period by echo stress.

METHoDs: Twenty two subject at beginning of sport activity (mean age 22 years) were followed for 4 years of regularly trained (3 times in a week) with clinical and echo-stress exame in a supine bike. REsULTS: MVP in the studied subject presented this characteristic : mild MR (+) in 56% moderate MR in 36% ( ++/+++) . After 4 years in trained subject the degree of MR at rest decreases progressively from moderate to mild in the 18% with 73% of inavaried; .

Observing PAP behaviour, in follow up period, we noted that if first of all the PAP values at rest are similar for each years, after a mild increase of maximal PAp value during stress but without statistical significativity, PAP values in all cases preserve normal range (lower of 30mmHg)

coNcLusIoNs: Our data show that echo-stress is a main excellent test for recognition and assessment MR and PAP in Athletes. In the subject studied at the end of follow up we noted that there is a higher % of MR improvement and a no significant increase of mean PAP . It seems that reqular exercise do not induced significativity emodinamical modification in the studied cases.

# Evaluation of Pulmonary Pressures during Exercise in Athletes. Echocardiography Study.

Andrea Moretti, M.C.R Vono, Marco Gianassi, Loira Toncelli, Laura Stefani, Paolo Manetti Valentina Di Tante. Azienda Ospedaliera Careggi-Medicine Sport centre-, Florence, Italy.

PURposE: Reqular training induces morpholoqical and functional cardiovascular modification.

The changes in pulmonary pressures during exercise were studied in a few cases, anc these studies show that pressures were higher in athletes than controls.

The aim of our study is the evaluation of the pulmonary pressure at rest and during exercise in a large population of athletes and untrained subjects.

METHoDS: We have studied 1915 conditioned athletes (38.1±18.5 vears) matched with 1898 controls (39.3 ±16 years). From transthoracic echocardiographic exame were obtained cardiac chambers dimensions, ejection fraction (EF), E peak, A peak, normal (LVM) and corrected for body surface area (LvMi) left ventricular mass. Besides, have been evaluated mean pulmonary pressure (MPAP) by using the acceleration method and systolic pulmonary pressure (SPAP) by tricuspidal requrgitation velocity (TVR) in a subgroup of subjects with detectable TRV (320 athletes and 300 controls), at rest and during cycle ergometer test (steps of 25-Watt incremental workloads are applied every 2 min).

REsuLTS: The resultes show that in athletes: EF = 64.8 ± 5.9 %, E peak = 76.5 ±18 m/sec. A peak = 47 ± 14.4 m/sec. LVM = 180.1 ± 91.8 ar. LVMi = 100 ± 51 ar/m? : in controls: EF = 62.8 ± 7.9%, E peak = 74.8 ± 19.1 m/sec, A peak = 58.4 ± 18.8 m/sec, LVM = 154.7 ± 55.3 gr, LVMi = 85.9 ± 30.7 gr/m² . So no significant differences of echocardiographic parameters have appeared between athletes and controls, even if there are significant differences of LVM and LVMi.

Mean rest PAP has been lower in athletes than in controls (16.6 ± 3 mmHg vs 17.4 ± 4.5 mmHg); no significant differences have been found in mean exercise PAP between athletes and controls (21.2 ± 4.1 mmHg vs 21.5 ± 5.9 mmHq). No significant differences have been found in rest and exercise TRV between athletes and controls (2.12 ± 0.4 - 2.18 ± 0.4m/sec vs 2.74 ± 0.4 - 2.68 ± 0.6m/sec), i.e. no differences have been found in rest and exercise systolic PAP in the two study groups (28.3 ± 6 - 40 ± 10.1 mmHg in athletes; 29 ± 7.4-38.7 ± 14 mmHg in controls).

coNcLusIoNs: This study, performed on a large population of conditioned athletes and untrained subjects, showed no significant differences in rest and exercise TRV or in systolic and mean PAP between the two groups

# Exercise Training Effects on Vascular Physiology and Biochemistry in PAD With and Without Diabetes

Earl H. Schwark, II, Elizabeth M. Miller, Brian Annex, Brian Duscha, Jennifer Robbins, Jason D. Allen. Duke University Medica! Center, Durham, NC. Email: j.d.allen@duke.edu

A disturbance in the ability to produce nitric oxide (No) during physiological stress may be an important limitation in vascular disease. Exercise training has been shown to up regulate NO production, improve endothelial function and increase VOzpeak

PURposE: To investigate (i) baseline differences in; and (i) supervised aerobic exercise effects on; physiological and biochemical markers of vascular health in subjects at risk (RFbaseline measures only) for peripheral arterial disease, with clinically diagnosed peripheral arterial disease (PAD) and with clinically diagnosed PAD plus type II diabetes mellitus (PADDM).

METHoDS: Subjects performed a maximal graded exercise test (GXT), with blood draws (5ml from the arm) at rest and at peak. Blood samples were stabilized, centrifuged, filtered and stored at -80°C. Plasma N-oxides (NOx) were measured by chemiluminescence using VCl in HCl at 94°C as the reductant. Brachial artery diameters were measured by ultrasound prior to, during and following 5min forearm occlusion. Exercise training consisted of supervised intermittent treadmill walking to claudication pain, 3x per week for 60min per session. Data is expressed as mean + standard deviation.

RESULTS: At baseline there was a stepwise relationship between RF. PAD and PADDM groups for VO2peak (24.7±6.6, 16.2±4.3, and 12.8± 0.9 ml/kg/min, respectively; p≤0.05) and BAFMD (5.7±3.1, 3.7±3.7, and 1.2+2.5% dilation, respectively; p<0.01). These variables were also correlated across groups (r²=0.11, p≤0.05). Additionally, NOx during maximal GXT increased in the RF group (+9%; p≤0.05) but not in the PAD (-16%) or PADDM (-2%) groups (see figure).

After 3 weeks of supervised exercise training both the PAD and PADDM grouns showed delayed onset of claudication pain (142+59 to 190+64 seconds p≤0.05 and 99+51 to 241+98 seconds p=0.07, respectively). Interestingly, only the PADDM group showed an increase in NOx during GXT (+5%, see figure), VOzpeak (16%) and brachial artery diameters (p<0.05) when compared to baseline

There were no changes for the PAD group in these variables until 3 months of training wher NOx during GXT increased by 4% (p<0.05, see figure) and vascular reactivity (BAFMD) increased by 50% (2.11 to 3.09%, p<0.05).

CoNcLusIoN: Our results suqgest that improvements in pain free walking times in both PAD and PADDM groups may occur via different biochemical and physiological pathways with different time courses.

## Cardiovascular

# Exercise Attenuates Effects of Diabetic Dyslipidemia on the Low Density Lipoproteome

Matt Richardson1, Joe Dixon², Michael Sturek², Frank Witzmann². Indiana University, Indianapolis, IN. 2Rutgers University, New Brunswick, NJ. Email: matrrich@iupui.edu

PURposE: We determined whether exercise training attenuates changes in the low density lipoprotein (LDL) proteome in diabetic dyslipidemia. Chronic endurance exercise has been demonstrated to reduce the incidence of coronary artery disease (CAD). However, the exact mechanism by which exercise exerts its cardioprotective effects is yet to be determined. A role for post-translationally modified (PTM) LDL has been proposed in the development of CAD. Diabetics are 3-4 times more likely to develop CAD and undergo enhanced LDL glycation, a PTM considered to accelerate atherogenesis. Interestingly, it has been documented that endurance athletes have lower levels of plasma LDL oxidation, one type of PTM. A decrease in the number and/or extent of LDL modifications may explain the cardioprotective effects of exercise. METHoDs: Yucatan male pigs were low fat fed healthy control (C), high fat/cholesterol fed and alloxan-induced diabetic (diabetic dyslipidemic, DD), and DD that were aerobically exercise trained (DDX). Increased coronary vascular constriction in a swine model of diabetic dyslipidemia (DD) was prevented by exercise training (DDX) without decreasing total LDL concentration (Mokelke et al. J. Appl. Physiol. 95:1179, 2003). Thus, we possess a nearly ideal system in which we can test the hypothesis that changes in the number of modified proteins or the extent of modification is most closely associated with exercise. The lipoproteins, isolated by FPLC from normolipidemic controls, DD, and DDX pigs, were analyzed by 2-D gel electrophoresis and identified by peptide mass fingerprinting. REsuLTS: Of 430 spots matched across all gel patterns analyzed, 171 spots were differentially expressed (p ≤ 0.05) and their identities determined. Of these, 22 changed with exercise (DDX vs. DD), of which only 2 decreased in abundance while the remaining 20 increased. Apolipoprotein E, an anti-atherogenic protein, demonstrated the greatest increase in abundance in the exercise trained group, DDX. To compare the extent of PTMs the charge modification index (CMI) of each LDL charge train was calculated. Numerous charge trains demonstrated increases in CMI in the DD, which likewise were attenuated with exercise training. One such charge train, phospholipase C, increased in overall abundance and CMI in DD and to a lesser extent in DDX, thus supporting our hypothesis. coNcLusIoNs: Similar effects in other LDL proteins suggest siqnificant differences in LDL protein modification, the nature of which requires the use of tandem mass spectrometric techniques in a subsequent investigation Supported by NIH RR13223, HL62552 (MS) and AFOSR 49620-002-1-0089 & FA9550-05- 1-0216 (FW).

## Cardiovascular

## Regional Endothelial Function and Plasma N-Oxides Discriminate PAD Health Status During Exericse Stress

Elizabeth M. Miller, Earl H. Schwark, Fred R. Cobb, Jennifer Robbins, Brian D. Duscha, Brian H. Annex, William E. Kraus, Jason D. Allen. Duke Univ. Medica/ Ctr., Durham, NC.

Nitric Oxide (NO) bioavailability has been shown to be reduced in the presence of CVD. A disturbance in the ability to produce No during cardiovascular stress may be an important limitation in PAD. Endothelial function of the brachial artery (via flow-mediated dilation BAFMD) has been shown to have predictive value for PAD and is NO dependent. We examined the link between whole body N-oxide (NOx) production before and following a maximal graded exercise test (GxT) and a regional physiologic measure of endothelial function (BAFMD) in subjects with PAD and age matched controls

Following a 12 hour fast and 1Omin supine rest brachial artery diameters were imagec prior to, during and following 5min of forearm occlusion and 0.4mg sublingual nitroglycerin. Subjects then performed a GXT. Blood (5ml) was drawn at rest (from the arm), and at exercise termination. The samples were mixed equally with 0.1M NaOH at 40, and centrifuged through a 10 kDa filter. The filtrates were stored at -70°C. NOx and measured by chemiluminesence utilizing vanadium chloride in hydrochloric acid at 94C as the reductant. Paired t-tests were used to evaluate within and between group differences. An alpha level of p≤0.05 was required for statistical significance.

The controls had higher ABI and VOzpeak than the PAD group. There were no differences in brachial diameters or plasma NOx at rest. Peak BAFMD (%change) following hyperemia (5.46%±2.99 vs. 2.49%±2.57; p≤0.01) was greater and occurred earlier (38.93sec ±11.79 vs. 47.33 sec ±11.81; p≤0.05) in the control than PAD subjects. This was despite no differences in blood flow, volume or estimated shear stress between the groups. Additionally, there were no differences in the arterial independent response to sublinqual NTG (0.4mg). Only the control group demonstrated significant increases in NOx during the GXT (8.8%) vs. PAD (-0.3%; p≤0.05).

This is the first study to link whole body NOx production with regional endothelial function in PAD and age-matched controls. Both measures were found to discriminate between populations. The plasma NOx samples were effectively drawn in a post exercise hyperemic state (from the arm following leg exercise) and are similar to the physioloqical measures of endothelial function following localized hyperemia. Coupling of these physiological and biochemical measures may provide additional insights into the mechanisms of altered reactivity and metabolic defects in atherosclerosis.

## Metabolic

## Chronic Exercise Training Increases Insulin Sensitivity in Obese Zucker Rats Potentially Via Hypothalamic Adiponectin Receptor 1

Amy L. Sindler², Sarah Morgan², Rafael A. Reyes², Jonathan M. Peterson², Jefferson C. Frisbee², Judy Muller-Delp², Randy W. Bryner, Stan M. Hileman². West Virginia University, Morgantown, WV. 2West Virginia University/University of The Andes, Morgantown/Mérida-Venezuela, WV.

Email: asindler@mix.wvu.edu

Adiponectin, a hormone secreted by adipose tissue, increases insulin sensitivity in skeleta muscle via adiponectin receptors AR1 and AR2. Recent evidence suggests adiponectin may act within the hypothalamus to alter insulin sensitivity in the periphery. Chronic exercise training has been shown to increase circulating adiponectin levels and AR1 expression in skeletal muscle, however the role of exercise on adiponectin receptors in the hypothalamus remains unknown.

puRposE: To examine the effects of exercise training on circulating adiponectin, mRNA expression of adiponectin in white adipose tissue and AR1 expression in both skeletal muscle and hypothalamus of obese Zucker rats.

METHoDs: Lean and obese Zucker rats ran on a treadmill for 50 min, 5 days a week or remained sedentary for 9 weeks. Fasting blood samples were collected at week 0, 3, 6, and 9 for measurements of blood glucose, insulin and adiponectin. Soleus muscle and hypothalami were collected for real-time RT-PCR assessment of AR1 and AR2 mRNA expression. White adipose tissue was collected for real-time RT-pCR assessment of adiponectin mRNA expression.

REsuLTS: Exercise training significantly lowered body weight in obese rats. Obese rats had higher blood glucose values than those of lean controls (p < .05), and exercise training for 9 weeks lowered blood alucose in obese rats (p < .05). Plasma insulin was sianificantly lower in the lean control rats compared to obese controls (.965 ± .36 Vs 4.642 ± .33 ng/ml; p < 0.05, respectively), and exercise training lowered plasma insulin levels in obese rats (p < 0.05). In both lean and obese groups plasma adiponectin was similar and was not altered by exercise training. Exercise training tended (p = 0.07) to increase adiponectir mRNA expression in white adipose tissue of both lean and obese rats. AR1 and AR2 mRNA expression in soleus muscle was similar in lean and obese controls, and were not altered with training; however exercise training increased AR1 mRNA expression in the hypothalamus of obese rats (p < 0.05).

coNcLusIoN: After 9 weeks of exercise training insulin sensitivity improved in obese Zucker rats. In obese Zucker rats, exercise training increased AR1 in the hypothalamus, but not within skeletal muscle. The exercise training-induced increase in insulin sensitivity is associated with an upreaulation of AR1 mRNA expression in the hypothamalus as circulating adiponectin, adiponectin mRNA, and skeletal muscle adiponectin receptor mRNA expression were unaffected by exercise training.

## Metabolic

# Exercise-Induced Changes in Abdominal Adipose Tissue: Effects of Training Intensity and Caloric Expenditure

Sophie E. Yeo, Patrick M. Kortebein, Amy D. Brown, Sarah R. Clary, William J. Evans. FACSM, Robert H. Coker, FACSM. University of Arkansas for Medical Sciences, Little Rock, AR.

Email: SYeo@uams.edu

PuRposE: To examine the influence of exercise training intensity and caloric expenditure on visceral (VI) and abdominal subcutaneous (AS) adipose tissue.

METHoDs: Following complete medical exams, 30 obese (BMI=31±1 kg/m²), older (61±3 years) subjects were randomized into four total groups. Two of these groups were under complete dietary control and included a exercise training-induced weight loss (EWL) group and an exercise training without weight loss (ENL) group at the same relative exercise intensity (ie., 50% of VO2peak). Caloric expenditure was matched at 2500 kcal/wk for EWL and ENL. However, ENL received no dietary compensation for their energy expenditure and their body weight was maintained. In accordance with minimal ACSM/CDC recommendations for exercise training, two additional groups expended 1000 kcal/week at 50% of VO2peak (MX), and 75% of VOzpeak (HX) without dietary control. In order to manipulate energy balance, EWL and ENL completed a dietary feeding study that included a 4 week weight maintenance period, a 12 week intervention period, a 2 week gradual refeeding period, and 4 week weight stabilization period. MX and HX were unlikely to lose weight based on 1000 kcal/week of exercise training and completed only the 12 week intervention study. Immediately prior to and following the exercise interventions, a whole body DEXA scan. and a CT scan at the L4-L5 level were performed.

RESULTS: Although there was a significant decrease in body weight in EWL, body weight did not change in ENL, MX, or HX. There were significant reductions in VI (-72±6 cm²) and AS (-31±7 cm²) adipose tissue in EWL. Although there was no change in AS adipose tissue in ENL, there was a significant reduction in VI (-30±10 cm?) adipose tissue. Despite the short intervention and low caloric expenditure, HX promoted a significant decline in VI (- 59±19 cm2) adipose tissue, while there were no changes in either abdominal fat depot in MX.

coNcLusIoN: Moderate intensity exercise training that results in weight loss represents the most effective therapeutic strategy for the reduction of VI and AS adipose tissue. In the absence of weight loss, moderate intensity exercise training utilizing high caloric flux (2500 kcal/ week) promoted a reduction in VI adipose tissue. Under conditions of low caloric flux (1o0o kcal/week) and no change in body weight, only high intensity exercise training reduced VI adipose tissue.

## Childhood Obesity: A Proposed Model for Intervention

Anne N. Graves1, Ravi R. Shankar, MD², Vivek Prasad, MD³, James Hoffman, MD. Clarian Health Partners, Indianapolis, IN. 2IU Pediatric Endocrinology, Indianapolis, IN. 3IU Pediatriacs, Indianapolis, IN. 4IU Pediatrics, Indianapolis, IN. Email: agraves1@clarian.org

INTRoDucTION: According to the 2005 Indiana Youth Risk Behavior Survey (YRBS) 15% of Indiana students are Overweight (Ow), a statistically significant increase from 11.5% recorded in 2003. The 2005 YRBS shows an additional 14.3% to be At Risk for Overweight (ARow), Since obesity in childhood and adolescence has been linked with excess morbidity both in pediatric as well as in adult age groups numerous programs have been instituted to prevent abnormal weight gain. By partnering with the Indianapolis Public Schools, anc Perry Township schools Clarian Health Partners carried out an obesity intervention among students within their health curriculum before offering it to a group of 8-13 vear-olds enrolled in summer camp. We report on the findings from this study here.

METHoDs: The course consisted of 10 one-hour sessions during the camp day. The Committed to Kids course was carried out over the course of 1O weeks during the summer of 2o06. Each session was divided into three topics: Nutrition Education, Behavior Management, and Physical Activity. Students went through a fitness assessment including: Height, Weight, BMI, Healthy Habit Assessments, as well as cardiovascular fitness and muscle endurance tests before and after the program.

REsuLTS: Complete data were available on 31/69 campers (20 males), of whom 7 (22%) were OW or AROw (BMI SDS > 1.5). While the group as a whole increased it's fitness level (cardiovascular and strength), healthy eating habits (consumptions of fruits and vegetables), amount of physical activity, and decreased TV watching siqnificantly, there was no difference in the weight/BMI/BMI-SDS. However, in the sub-group of 7 children who were OW or AROw, the program did result in significant decrease in weight (56.2±2.7 vs 55.6±2.6 Kg, p= .029), BMI (26.6±1.4 vs 26.3±1.3 Kg.m²2, p=.027) and BMI-SDS (2.06±.09 vs 2.04±.09, p=.037). We estimate the cost of the program to be approximately \$ 35.00 per participant.

coNcLusioNs: These data suggest that this program can be effectively incorporated into a camp curriculum. Questions that remain to be answered Changes in Parameters Before (Pre) and After (Post) intervention

## Metabolic

# Effects of Exercise Training in Walk Energy Economy are Associated with Change in Regional Body Composition

Elvis Á. Carnero, Maria José Valamatos, Ronei Pinto, Pedro Mil-Homens, Luís B. Sardinha. Faculty of Human Movement, Lisbon, Portugal. Email: ecarnero@fmh.utl.pt

PuRposE: The aim of this study was primarily to examine the training effects in oxyger uptake (VO2) changes relative to a standard load (energy economy, EE). Secondly, we explored body composition predictors that might explain the potential energy expenditure changes.

METHoDs: An exercise training protocol was developed including walking and resistance training exercises and was performed by 49 previously sedentary and overweight women (age, 39.4±0.7 yrs; BMI, 29.0±0.3 kg.m2; FM%, 41.9±0.8%), during a period of 5 months. Body composition and EE assessment were performed at baseline (M0) and after the training period (M5). Body weight was measured to the nearest 0.1 kg and height was measured to the nearest 0.1 cm. Total and regional fat mass (FM), percent fat mass (%FM) and fat-free mass (FFM) were estimated with Dual-energy x-Ray Absorptiometry (DXA. Hologic QDR 1500, MA, USA; software QDR for Windows XP 12.0). The walk EE was estimated with an incremental protocol of exercise, with three minutes in each stage, at a constant velocity of 5.6 km.h"1; the grade increased by 2.5% between stages. The VO at each stage was measured by a breath-by-breath system (Quark b² Cosmed®, Italy). VO was controlled for FFM and integrated minute-by-minute, and the last steady-state minute in each stage was selected for data analysis. Differences between baseline and after intervention were calculated for the selected variables.REsuLTS

Paired T-test showed a significance difference between the VO2ffm M5 and VO2ffm M0 in all stages (-7.2 ml.kgfm".min; p<0.001). In the stepwise regression analysis, the change in trunk FFM (TrFFM), and the lower limbs fat mass (LoLsFM), were the variables that entered in the final model, explaining 27.9% of the EE change. β-unstandardized coefficients for intercept, both slopes of TrFFM, and LoLsFM difference were respectively -3.46, -0.98, and 1.23; p < 0.001. The standard error of the estimate of the model was ±3.64 ml.kgffm-1.min . Therefore, some regional body composition changes between M0 and M5 predicted EE change.

## CONCLUSIONS

The main finding of this study is that training induced EE alteration in sedentary overweight women, is associated with regional body composition changes. The observed increases in the trunk fat-free mass, and the decrease of fat mass in the lower limbs, have influenced the energy expenditure of the walking exercise protocol. A novel contribution of this study is that trunk fat-free mass is a predictor of EE change. Therefore, adjustments in the exercise load must be performed when changes in theses variables are observed

34

## Metabolic

# Effect of Aerobic Exercise Training on Expression of Genes Involved in Ceramide Metabolism

Ira J. Smith, Kim M. Huffman, William E. Kraus, FACSM. Duke University, Durham, NC Email: ira4@duke.edu

Insulin resistance is a central pathophysiological characteristic of type 2 diabetes mellitus, and metabolic syndrome. Ceramide metabolism may play an important role in insulin resistance since ceramide content negatively correlates with insulin signaling in human skeletal muscle. We hypothesized that aerobic exercise training previously shown tc improve insulin sensitivity would also alter expression of two genes involved in ceramide metabolism, acid ceramidase (AC) and serine palmitoyltransferase (SP), in skeletal muscle. A corollary hypothesis was that changes in AC and SP gene expression would correlate with improved insulin sensitivity following exercise training. Women with metabolic syndrome were randomly assigned to either a sedentary control group (n = 5) or one of three aerobic exercise-training paradigms (n = 5 per group). Six months of aerobic exercise training significantly increased both AC (P = 0.04) and SP (P = 0.03) gene expression. Though not statistically significant, expression of both genes tended to decrease over the six-month period in the sedentary control group (P = 0.12). When distributed by exercise training group, trends in gene expression changes were markedly similar to our previously reported changes in insulin sensitivity. No correlations existed between change in gene expressior and change in insulin sensitivity, possibly due to the small sample size. Nonetheless, these preliminary data suggest that altered AC and/or SP gene expression is indicative of and may contribute to the worsening insulin sensitivity noted with lack of physical exercise and improved insulin sensitivity with aerobic exercise training. Therefore additional experiments should be conducted to increase the power of the current investigation. and these follow: up studies should include male subjects to evaluate these findings in men. Supported by NHLBI: R01 HL057354-06 and NIA: AG00029-30.

## SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 30, 2006 • POSTER SESSION 3

## Metabolic

# Influence of Exercise Intensity on O2 Consumption and Substrate Utilization During and After Resistance Exercise

Scott Mazzetti, Matthew Douglass, Aaron Yocum, Abby Peters. Anderson University, Anderson, IN. Email: samazzetti@anderson.edu

## PURPOSE:

The purpose of this study was to examine the influence of resistance exercise intensity (explosive vs slow muscle actions; and moderate vs heavy loading) on oxygen consumption and substrate use during and after resistance exercise.

## METHODS:

Nine men (20±2.5yrs) performed three exercise sessions using a plate-loaded sguat machine and one control session (CoN) in random order (1/wk). Subiects performed the concentric portion of squats either explosively (EXPL) or slowly over 2s (SLOw) for 4 sets of 8 repetitions (reps) with 60% of 1-RM; or explosively for 6 sets of 4 reps with 80% of 1 RM (HEAVYEXPL), and then sat for 1hr. Eccentric rep time (2s each), total work (sets X reps x load), squat range-of-motion, and rest intervals between sets (9os) were identical for all squat protocols. Expired air was collected continuously for 20min before, during, and 1hr following exercise, and for 1.5hr during CoN. Blood samples (25ul) were collected before, immediately after, and 15, 30, 45, and 60min after each protocol, and analyzed immediately for blood lactate concentration.

## RESULTS:

The relative rate of oxygen consumption (ml/kg/min) was significantly greater (p≤0.05) during, and 5, 10, and 15min after EXPL compared to SLOW and HEAVYEXPL, despite greater blood lactate concentration after SLOW squats.Explosive muscle actions (EXPL and HEAVYEXPL) induced a more rapid increase in fat oxidation after exercise, with HEAVYEXPL > SLOW and EXPL during exercise and 5min after, and EXPL > SLOW at 10 and 15min after exercise.

## CONCLUSIONS:

In summary, squats performed with explosive muscle actions induced a greater increase in oxygen consumption, and carbohydrate and fat oxidation than slow muscle actions when otherwise identical exercise protocols were performed. Thus, by simply performing the concentric portion of resistance exercises explosively, recreational exercisers can increase their energy expenditure which may enhance weight loss.

## Metabolic

## Acute Inflammation and Intramyocellular Lipids

Lan Chi T. Luu², Bradley Newcomer², Willem ].S de Villiers², Trevor A. Winter², Michael B. Reid. University of Kentucky, Lexington, KY. University of Alabama at Birmingham, Birmingham, AL Email: LCLUU2@UKY.EDU

## PURPOSE:

Intramyocellular lipids (IMcL) are lipid depositions in myocytes. Considered to be part of normal physiology, IMCL act as an added source of energy during strenuous exercise However, in pathophysiological conditions, including obesity and diabetes, elevated IMCLs demonstrate strong association to insulin resistance. IMcL concentrations are regulated by adipokines. Leptin administration increases fatty acid oxidation and reduces IMcL resulting in increased insulin sensitivity in both lean and obese rodents. Althouah resistin and TNFa are implicated in reducing insulin sensitivity, their involvement in IMCL accumulation is controversial. Furthermore, the relationship between leptin, resistin, TNFa , and IMCL are virtually unexamined in systemic inflammatory disease state. We hypothesized that in an acute exacerbation of Crohn's disease, IMCL levels are increased in conjunction with increased circulating TNFa , resistin, and leptin levels .

## METHODS:

15 active CD patients were matched to 13 controls based on age, gender, race and BM] Serum levels of CRP, TNFa , resistin, leptin, and leukocytes were measured. Levels of IMCL were quantified using proton magnetic resonance spectroscopy (HMRS) and jMRUI 2-2

## software.

## RESULTS:

The average age of subjects was 35 years (± 2.3 SE) and the mean BMI was 22.2 (± 0.4) ka/m². Compared to controls, active CD subjects had increased levels of CRP (2.20 mg/dl ± 2.3 vs. 0.48 ± 0.03, p≤0.03), TNFa (11.9 pg/ml ± 7.1 vs. 4.6 ± 1.9, p≤0.02), and leukocytes (4.8 k/uL ± 0.5 vs. 2.8 ± 0.2, p≤0.004). There was a decreased in IMCL in CD subiects compared to controls (2 8 ± 0 5 vs 6 0 ± 1. 3. p=0 022). Total cholesterol was also decreased in CD subjects compared to controls (134.4 mg/dL ± 7.5 vs. 189.8 ± 6.1, p=0.001).

## CONCLUSIONS:

During active CD, acute inflammation does not appear to increase IMCL levels. Although not significant, preliminary analyses suggest leptin may be slightly reduced and resistin may increase.

37

## Metabolic

# Effect of Morning Exercise on Daily Post-prandial Triglcerides: Comparsion Between Women and Men

Tracy J. Horton², Teresa A. Sharp². Sarah A. Stotz1, Daniel H. Bessesen². 1University of Colorado at Denver and Health Sciences Center, Denver, Co. 2Denver Health Medical Center, Denver, CO.

Email: tracy.horton@uchsc.edu

PuRPosE: An increased post-prandial triglyceride (PPTG) excursion is an independent risk factor for coronary heart disease, therefore, evaluating factors that increase or decrease this parameter is important. Women reportedly have a lower PpTG after a sinale mea compared to men whereas moderate exercise decreases PpTG after consumption of a single, very high fat meal. The aims of this study, therfore, were to a) determine if a single bout of morning exercise can significantly decrease PPTGs over an entire day, when consuming meals of a more typical macronutrient composition and b) to determine if there is a sex difference in the daily PpTG excursion with and/or without exercise.

METHoDs: Non-obese (BMI 20-27 kg/m²), untrained, healthy, women (n=11) and men (n=12) were recruited and matched for fasting TG levels (101 ±27 and 100 ±14 mg/dl, respectively). Subjects completed an exercise and a rest study day (randomized order) separated by 3-4 weeks. Prior to each study day, subjects consumed a controlled diet (5days) and abstained from planned exercise (60 hrs). After an overnight stay on the clinical research unit, subjects commenced testing at 7:15 am after an overnight fast. On the exercise day, subjects walked on a treadmill for 60 mins at 60% VO peak and on the rest day, subjects rested for the same time period. Breakfast was consumed at 8:30 am, with lunch and dinner at 12:30 pm and 5:30 pm, respectively. Macronutrient composition of each meal was 34% fat, 15% protein and 51% carbohydrate. Subjects remained resting from 8:30 am onwards and energy intake was calculated to meet energy requirements of the corresponding day. Blood samples were drawn before exercise (or rest) and continued throughout the remainder of the day (total of 26 time points).

REsuLTS: The integrated area under the curve (IAUC) for total TGs over the entire day was significantly lower on the exercise vs rest day in both woren (7766 ± 2977 vs 22947 ±2979 mg/dl/840 mins, respectively P < 0.0001) and men (20196 ± 5660 vs 29721 ±5252 mg/dl/840 mins). There was an overall significant sex difference in the daily PPTG excursion (IAUC, P = 0.05), mainly due to the lower response in women vs men on the exercise day (P=0.07). In both sexes, the difference in the PPTG excursion on the exercise vs rest day was greatest after lunch (P= 0.0003 women, P=0.03 men) and dinner (P<0.0oo1 and P = 0.05, respectively). There were no sex or day differences in the daily insulin and glucose excursions.

coNcLustoNs: In conclusion, a single bout of moderate exercise can reduce the daily pPTG response when consuming meals of a typical macronutrient composition, and this response tends to be more pronounced in women vs men.

# Exercise Training- versus Caloric Restriction-Induced Weight Loss: Effects on Hepatic and Peripheral Insulin Sensitivity

Robert H. Coker, FAcsM. Sophie E. Yeo. Rick H. Williams, Patrick M. Kortebein, William J. Evans, FACSM. University of Arkansas for Medical Sciences, Little Rock, AR. Email: cokerrobert@uams.edu

PuRPosE: To examine the influence of matched weight loss using either exercise training or caloric restriction on hepatic and peripheral insulin resistance.

METHoDS: Following medical exams, 17 obese (BMI=31±1 kg/m²), older (58±2 years) ze  ex   % V (EWL), caloric restriction-induced weight loss (CR) or a sedentary control (CN) group Utilizing an experimental paradigm to ensure a matched caloric deficit (2500 kcal/week) in EWL and CR, subjects completed a dietary feeding study that included a 4 week weight maintenance period, a 12 week intervention period, a 2 week gradual re-feeding period, and 4 week weight stabilization period. Immediately following the weight maintenance and weight stabilization periods designed to ensure caloric balance, pre- and post-testing procedures were performed, and included a DEXA scan and a CT scan at the L4-L5 level. In addition, an octreotide (60 ng•kg1•min²), glucagon (0.65 ng•kg min1) multi-stage insulin (0.25 and 1.0 mU•kg•min1) infusion (MSI) was performed. During the MSI euglycemia was maintained by a variable rate infusion of 20% dextrose spiked with [6,6 lucos Endogenous ucos productin glucose  was measure  baseline an during the MSI using a primed, multi-stage infusion [0.22 (first stage) and 0.44 (second stage) μmol•kg"1•min"1] of [6,62H2]glucose. Hepatic insulin action (HIA) was calculated as the % of glucose R suppressed by mild hyperinsulinemia (ie., elicited by the 0.25 mU•kg insulin infusion Insulin-stimulated glucose disposal (ISGD) was calculated y subtracting glucose R from 20% dextrose infusion rate required to maintain euglycemia during the second stage of the MSI.

REsuLTS: Body weight decreased similarly in EWL (-5.3±0.5 kg) and CR (-5.8±0.9 kg), while there was an increase in CN (2.3±0.5 kg). Weight loss-induced reductions in visceral fat were significantly greater in EWL (-72±6 cm2) compared to CR (-34±8 cm²), while there was a sliqht increase in CN (8±4 cm²). Also, improvements in HIA were twice as high in EWL (+16%) compared to CR (+7%). Improvements in ISGD were similar in EWL (+2.27±0.5 mg•kgFFM-1•min-1) and CR (+2.30±0.1 mg•kgFFM-1•min-1), and HIA and ISGD declined in CN

coNcLusIoN: EWL promoted a greater reduction in visceral fat and superior improvements in HIA compared to CR. While increases in ISGD were similar in EWL and CR, the superior improvements in HIA with EWL suggest that weight loss resulting from increased phvsical activity may promote areater overall therapeutic benefits than weiaht loss resulting from caloric restriction.

## Metabolic

# The Effect of Limb Blood Flow on Sensorimotor Responses in Adults with Type 2 Diabetes

Donald W. Zakutansky, David M. Koceja, Janet P. Wallace, FACSM. Indiana University, Bloominaton, IN. Email: dzakutan@indiana.edu

BAckGRouND: Diabetic peripheral neuropathy may be caused by neuronal ischemia and hypoxemia caused by hyperglycemia-induced decreases in neurovascular flow . In humans, H-reflex methodology allows one to localize the effect of ischemia to a single synapse in the spinal cord and may help to uncover how ischemia may produce changes in sensorimotor function, H-reflex and motor threshold decreases with ischemia have been reported in young adults, indicating more excitable sensory and motor nerves (Zakutansky et al 2oo4). These changes were accompanied by a decrease in H-reflex amplitudes with ischemia. To date. protocols utilizing the H-reflex to examine blood flow implications have not been reported in diabetes.

puRPosE: To examine the effect of limb blood flow on peripheral nerve function in adults with type 2 diabetes and apparently healthy controls. It was hypothesized that acute ischemia would increase excitability of sensory and motor nerves while an increase in blood flow would decrease excitability of the sensory and motor nerves

METHoDs: Ten adults with type 2 diabetes and 10 age- and BMI-matched controls participated in the study. Soleus H-reflex and motor recruitment curves were determined at baseline, during 10 minutes of ischemia by femoral artery occlusion, and after a 10- minute bout of leg exercise.

REsuLTS: At baseline. the H-threshold occurred at 77.44 ± 1.12% and 92.23 ± 0.04% (mean ± SE) of MTc for the diabetic and control groups, respectively. During ischemia, the H-threshold occurred at 72.44 ± 7.19% and 88.79 ± 7.80% of MTc for the diabetic anc control groups, respectively. Following exercise, the H-threshold occurred at 64.44 ± 8.47% or 94.93 ± 4.30% of MTc for the diabetic and control groups, respectively, which significantly differed from baseline. Hmax/Mmax ratios were significantly reduced with acute ischemia for controls (4.2 %) and the diabetics (25.1%). These changes were due to a decrease in Hmax during acute ischemia with no change in Mmax. Post-exercise, the Hmax/Mmax returned to baseline for controls while the diabetic group remained significantly lower than at control. Post-exercise, H-reflex and motor responses were hypoexcitable with an accompanying hyperemia for controls. In the diabetic group, the post-exercise period resulted in a hyperexcitable H-reflex and motor response.

coNCLUsIoN: Acute ischemia decreases H-reflex thresholds in adults with diabetes anc exercise-induced increases in blood flow further decrease these thresholds. These responses are markedly more pronounced than those of their non-diabetic peers and are accompanied by changes in the motorneuron pool.

# Changes in Regional Body Composition after 6 Months of Supervised Resistance Training

Bryan K. Smith, Jeff J. Honas, Richard A. Washburn, Joseph E. Donnelly, FACSM. University of Kansas, Lawrence, KS. Email: smithbr@ku.edu

Few studies have shown resistance training (RT) to be an effective treatment for weight loss, howeyer; RT may result in both decreased total body and abdominal (trunk) fat with no change in body weight due to increased lean tissue. Reductions in percent body fat (BF) and trunk fat tissue have been associated with decreased cardiovascular disease risk.

PURPOSE: To determine the effects of 6 months of RT on body weight, BF, total and regional fat tissue, and total body and regional lean tissue in young men and women.

METHODS: Fifty-five overweight $( \mathsf { B M I } = \mathsf { \bar { 2 } 7 } . 3 \pm 2 . 8 ~ \mathsf { k g } / \mathsf { m } ^ { 2 } ) ,$ weight stable (< 2.2 kg weight change in last 6 months), sedentary (< 500 Kcal/wk in leisure time physical activity) individuals (25 females (F), 30 males (M), age = 20.5 ± 2.6 yrs) were randomly assigned to RT (n = 32, 15 F, 17 M) or control (CON, n = 23, 10 F, 13 M). RT consisted of 1 set of 9 weight stack machine exercises (6 upper and 3 lower body exercises) at 3-6 repetition maximum, under direct supervision, 3 days/wk for 6 months. Body weight (calibrated scale), BF, total and reqional fat tissue, and total and reqional lean tissue (dualenergy X-ray absorptiometry) were assessed at baseline and following 6 months of RT. Data for M and F were analyzed separately.

REsuLTS: In M, there were no significant between group changes for body weight (RT = +3.6 ± 4.6 kg, CON = +3.0 ± 2.6 kg (p = 0.64)), BF (RT = +1.08 ± 3.70%, CON = +2.71 ± 2.38% (p = 0.16)), and total fat tissue (RT = +0.95 ± 4.38 kg, CON = +3.79 ± 4.08 kg (p = 0.25)). However, significant between group changes were observed in M for total lean tissue (RT = +1.49 ± 2.46 kg, CON = -0.45 ± 1.08 kg (p = 0.008)). In F, there were nc significant between group changes for body weight (RT = +0.7 ± 4.2 kg, CON = +0.7 ± 1.9 kg (p = 0.97). However, significant between group changes were observed in F for BF (RT = -1.32 ± 3.1%, CON = +0.76 ±1.6% (p = 0.040)), leg lean tissue (RT = +0.56 ± 0.71 kg, CON = -0.21 ± 0.53 kg (p=0.049)), trunk fat tissue (RT = - 0.68 ± 2.13 kg, CON = + 0.91 ± 1.21 kg (p=0.026)), and total body lean tissue (RT = +1.40 ± 1.23 kg, CON = - 0.14 ± 1.23 kq (p=0.006)).

coNcLusIoN. These results suggest that in young, overweight, sedentary F, 6 months of supervised RT results in significant changes in body composition that are associated with decreased cardiovascular disease risk without significant changes in body weight. In M, RT resulted in an significant increase in total body lean tissue but did not result in significant changes in body weight, BF, or total body fat tissue suggesting a potential gender difference in the body composition response to RT in young, sedentary, overweight individuals. Supported by NIH Grant DK62832.
